Preface
This master thesis project has been carried out by Mads Stevnsborg in collaboration with
the PROSYS Research Centre, situated at the department of Chemical- and Biochemical
Engineering, at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The project was initiated
on the 1st of February 2021, and submitted on the 25th of June 2021, and it accounts
for a combined workload of 30 ECTS points. The project has been supervised by Jakob
Kjøbsted Huusom, Jens Abildskov and Jess Bjørn Rasmussen.
I would like to express my deepest gratitude to all my supervisors for their guidance
and technical assistance in helping me perform this master’s thesis project. Additionally,
I would like thank my partner Sara Enevoldsen, who’s love and support, got me through
the project in one piece.
Mads Stevnsborg (s164083)
Date
i
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This thesis is a theoretical study that investigates possible design methods to improve the
selection of the vapor flow period duration, which is used in periodic reactive distillation
processes. The investigation employs a verified simulation model, developed using the
most recently published mathematical system interpretation for periodic reactive distil-
lation. The operational effect of the vapor flow period duration is evaluated for three
different case studies. This includes the production of methyl acetate, dimethyl ether
(DME) and methyl tert-buthyl ether (MTBE) which are all equilibrium limited reaction
systems.
It is concluded that if the production capacity and vapor flow rate from the reboiler
are constant, the vapor flow period duration can be arbitrarily selected for heterogeneous
reaction systems. A short period cycles with a small feed amount in the liquid flow period
is in this case equivalent, to a column operating at long cycles with a larger feed amount
in the liquid flow period. The observed effect is independent of the rate of reaction. The
deviation observed for the homogeneous MTBE system is most likely attributed to the
inclusion of a liquid holdup dependency in the rate expression. To further improve the
selection methods for the vapor flow period duration for heterogeneous case systems, the
liquid holdup must be included in the system model.
The operational effects of the vapor flow rate from the reboiler and reflux ratio
are also evaluated using the dimensionless Damköhler number. This analysis, employed
a Damköhler number computed by interpretating each tray as an independent batch
reactor. The produced results which lacked suﬀicient and consistent correlation between
the operation and Damköhler number to formulate a novel design strategy applicable for
arbitrary systems. A more accurate interpretation of the dimensionless parameter for a
periodic reactive distillation, could potentially be used in future short-cut designs.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Resumé
Dette speciale er et teoretisk studie, som undersøger mulige design metoder, der kan
anvendes til at forbedre valget af periodetidens længde for periodisk reaktive distiller-
ingsprocesser. Studiet anvender en verificeret simuleringsmodel, der bygger på de senest
publiserede matematiske udtryk, der besrkiver den periodiske drift af reaktive distilla-
tionskolonner. Påvirkningen af forskellige periodelængder er undersøgt for tre forskellige
kemiske systemer. Disse systemer inkluderer produktionen af methyl acetate, diemthyl
ether (DME) og methyl tert-buthyl ether (MTBE), som alle er ligevægtsbegrænsede.
Det konkluderes, at hvis produktionkapaciteten og dampstrømmen fra opdamperen
er konstante, så kan periodelængden vælges arbitrært for heterogene reaktionssystemer.
Dette betyder, at en kolonne som opereres ved kort periodetid med en lille fødemængde
i væskeflow perioden, er ækvivalent med en kolonne, som opereres ved lang periode-
tid med en større fødemængde i væskeflow perioden. Denne observation var umildbart
uafhængtig af reaktionens hastighed. Afvigelsen for det homogene MTBE system skyldes
formentlig, at reaktionsudtrykket afhænger af væskemængden på trinnet. For at forbedre
metoderne mht. til valg af periodelængde for de heterogene systemer, må der inkluderes
en afhængighed af væskemængden på trinnet i deres dynamiske modeller.
Dampflowet fra opdamperen og reflux ratioens inflydelse på operationen er også un-
dersøgt ved anvendelse af det dimmensionsløse Damköhler tal. Analysen anvender en
definition af Damköhler tallet, der antager, at hvert trin opfører sig ækvivalent med
isoleret batch reaktor. De producerede resultater er ikke tilstrækkeligt konsistente mht.
korrelationer mellem Damköhler tallet og kolonneoperationen til at en formel og gener-
aliseret design strategi kan formuleres for arbitrære systemer. En mere repræsentativ
formulering af Damköhlertallet for periodisk reaktiv distillering kan muligvis anvendes i
fremtidige design strategier.
iii

Chapter 1
Introduction
Separation processes are an essential part of any chemical facility and they constitute a
type of operational unit that is responsible for purifying streams of mixed components.
These operations are extensively used in the chemical industry to remove hazardous chem-
icals from waste streams, or to improve product quality. There exists many different sep-
aration methods, with distillation being the predominant methodology implemented in
industry today. Currently, there are more than 40.000 distillation columns operating at
commercial scale worldwide. As a result, almost all consumer products contain elements,
which at some point in the production chain, passed through a distillation column. Dis-
tillation is an old technology, which has remained popular due to its effective operation
and simple implementation [2]. However, one major disadvantage in employing distil-
lation, in its basic form is that it often results in very low thermodynamic eﬀiciencies,
reported between 5%-18%, depending on the specific separation. Due to the extensive
utilization of the process in industry, combined with the generally low energy eﬀiciency,
distillation alone accounts for 40% of the yearly energy consumption within the entire
chemical industry [2, 3, 4]. The utilities and raw materials are the dominating expenses
in a chemical facility sometimes amount to 90% of the lifetime expenses for a facility,
even when the capital investment for physical equipment is included [5].
The chemical industry has become an integrated part of modern society, producing
a vast portfolio of feed-stocks chemicals and finished goods used in plastics, construc-
tion materials, fertilizer, pharmaceuticals and anything in-between [5]. With the rapid
growth of Asian industries the chemical sector has been booming since the 2000s, almost
doubling in global production capacity by 2017. The trend is projected to continue, pre-
dicting that the global revenue stream will double from AC3.47 −AC6.6 trillion (2017) by
2030. If pharmaceutical products are included in the sales total, the combined revenue
of the chemical sector amounts to AC5.68 trillion (2017), making it the second largest
manufacturing industry in the world [6].
Growing environmental concerns, regarding global warming, and loss of natural habi-
tat has compelled the international community of the United Nations to realize an ambi-
tious initiative, publishing 17 sustainability development goals (SDG) to be achieved by
1
==PAGE:14==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
2030. Among these initiatives are specific demands which force the chemical industry to
rethink their production paradigms [6, 3]. Great innovations have already been diligently
developed since the 1990s, which have succeeded in reducing the energy intensity of the
sector by 59.7% [3]. Despite the impressive advancements in the last two decades, the
chemical sector still accounts for 30% of the total global industrial energy consumption,
thus continuing to be the greatest energy consuming industry [5]. Recently published
data from the international energy agency (IEA) show that the fraction of a countries
energy consumption, used in the chemical sector, is generally greater for westernized
countries such as the European Union and the United States, reporting a 39% and 44%
total industrial energy consumption respectively [7, 8]. Combined with the rapid increase
in industrial capacity for Asian countries such as China and India, interests for energy
reducing innovations within the chemical industry has never been higher. The global
research and development (R&D) spending has therefore been increased to AC39.4 billion
(2017) [6].
It is perhaps not surprising that distillation has received much attention in recent
decades, and great efforts are still being made to improve its energy-eﬀiciency. Since
the separation method is an integrated part of the modern chemical industry small ad-
vancements can have huge implications in energy savings, which not only translates into
lod operational expenses, but also significant reductions in green house gas emissions
produced within the chemical sector [2, 3].
1.1
Advanced Distillation
Distillation in its basic form is a separation process, which exploits the differences in
boiling point temperatures of chemical species in a mixture, to gradually evaporate the
most volatile components. The purity of the volatile species, often denoted the light com-
ponents, increase up through the column. Simultaneously, the purity of the least volatile
species, often denoted the heavies, increase down towards the bottom of the column.
Inside the distillation column, a vapor phase flows, from the bottom, up through the
equipment, while a liquid phase simultaneously trickles down counter-currently. The two
phases share a contact surface as they move past each other, which allows for inter-phase
mass- and energy transfer to occur. Once the vapor reaches the top of the column, it is
either partially or completely condensed. A portion of the now liquid distillate is returned
to column, commonly referred to as the liquid reflux. A similar operation is conducted
for the liquid phase reaching the bottom of the column, where a portion of the bottoms
is reboiled, and returned to the column as a vapor flow [9]. Going forward, the distil-
lation process in its basic form will be referred to as a conventional distillation process.
A schematic of the distillation column and three column internals for the conventionally
operated distillation column is illustrated in Figure 1.1.
The reboil- and reflux ratios are operating parameters, often manipulated to obtain
a desired top and bottom purity. They denote the fraction of vapor condensed in the top,
and fraction of liquid entering the reboiler, which is returned to the column relative to
the amount leaving as the distillate- and bottoms products. The operating reflux- and
boilup ratios are mostly determined by the size of the column, and the relative volatility
2
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
between the species in the mixture.
If the relative volatility between the species are
small, they have similar boiling point temperatures and become diﬀicult to separate. In
this case large reflux- and boilup ratios are often required to achieve purity and recovery
constraints. The large operating ratios correspond to a high energy consumption relative
to the product leaving the column, which is the origin of the low thermodynamic eﬀiciency.
Usually the reboiler employ a high-quality heat source in the form of high-pressure steam
generated from a gas- or oil fired boiler. In the condenser, the hot vapor is cooled, often
using ambient temperature water. As a result, the vast majority of high-quality energy
investment in the reboiler is extracted in the condenser as a liquid stream of 45-60 °C.
While this stream still contains a large portion of the energy investment in the reboiler,
it is diﬀicult to practically employ it in other parts of the chemical facility. Often this
flow is therefore rejected to the environment instead [4].
Figure 1.1: (left) Schematic of a conventional distillation column, with key parameters used
in design and operation denoted. (right) Schematic for three different tray designs commonly
employed in most distillation columns, (a) sieve/bubble cap trays with down-comers, (b) un-
structured packaging and (c) structured packaging.

==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
The study and development of improved- and advanced distillation belongs to re-
search within the field of Process Intensification (PI). This relatively new paradigm within
chemical engineering research encompass development, in either methodologies or process
equipment, which improves the overall eﬀiciency of chemical operations [10]. Early defi-
nitions of PI, focused only on development related to reducing the equipment size while
maintaining a comparable productivity. More recent definitions have broaden the term
to include advancements that improve general energy eﬀiciency [11].
The PI improvements applied in distillation often pertain are often implemented as
alternative to the column design presented in Figure 1.1. The majority of PI research into
distillation columns therefore either attempt to use the low quality, medium temperature,
stream via. heat-integration [4], or to improve the overall column separation eﬀiciency to
require less utility. An example would be to utilize an alternative column configurations
to reach the same product purity targets [2, 12, 13, 14]. In this thesis two such methods
are investigated in detail, namely reactive- and periodic distillation.
1.1.1
Reactive Distillation
In most chemical facilities, are the reactions carried out in dedicated reaction vessels. The
exiting flows of mixed products, unreacted reactants and general impurities are then sent
downstream to dedicated separation processes for product purification. Reactive distilla-
tion (RD), also referred to as catalytic distillation, combines the reaction and separation
process, by carrying out the synthesis inside a distillation column [15]. The resulting syn-
ergies, between the chemical reaction and separation, can result in a significant increase
in the overall energy eﬀiciency [16]. Examples of the possible synergies that reactive distil-
lation provide, is continued product removal and energy contributions from the reaction.
These synergies have been shown to especially improve equilibrium restricted reactions,
where un-reacted raw materials would otherwise have to be recycled multiple times, in
order to reach adequate conversion rates. Additionally, it has been demonstrated, that it
is possible to mitigate azeotropes, using the reaction to push across the azeotrope point
[15, 17]. These synergies allow for a reduction in equipment size, or utility consumption
during operation.
The concept of catalytic distillation has existed for a long time, but gained significant
traction within the industry, 50 years ago.
Since the 1980s the technology has been
extensively developed and implemented in commercial production scale.
Today it is
regarded as a PI success story, with more than 200 operating columns at production
capacities between 100 - 3000 kton/year [15, 16, 18]. Perhaps the most famous example
of using reactive separation, is the production of methyl acetate [19] [16].
CH3OH + CH3COOH
H+
−−⇀
↽−−CH3COOCH3 + H2O
The process, known as the Eastman process, is a prime example of the potential
increase in energy eﬀiciency, gained from the synergistic relationship possible in reactive
distillation units. The conventional process setup, and the patented Eastman process
are illustrated side-by-side in Figure 1.2. By switching from the conventional process
4
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
to reactive distillation, both the operational energy consumption and capital investment
have been reduced by approximately 80%, for similar production capacities [16].
The prospect of implementing reactive distillation however, depend greatly on the
specific chemical system. Perhaps evident from the section above, the methodology is
bound by thermodynamic limitations which narrows the range of feasible systems [16].
It is an obvious limitation that the volatility of products and raw materials must differ
significantly for distillation aspects to be effective. Otherwise, the raw materials and
products will end up together in the bottom and/or top sections, where additional sep-
aration is required. Alternatively, the column will become very large, if the chemical
reaction is very slow, which limits the economic gain. Additionally, the pressure and
temperature conditions for the reaction and distillation operations must be adequately
similar, to allow for a functional operation. Finally the equilibrium constant for the prod-
uct cannot be too small. With decreased aﬀinity for the product side, the economic and
energy benefits diminishing until the process becomes unattractive [20].
Figure 1.2: Schematic of the process flow diagrams for a conventional reactive process setup (a)
and a reactive distillation setup (b) for methyl acetate production. The implemented synthesis
convert methanol and acetic acid to mehtyl acetate and water, catalyzed by sulphuric acid [16].
A reactive distillation column ´usually resemble the conventional configuration de-
picted in Figure 1.1, with a liquid and vapor phase moving simultaneously and counter-
currently through the equipment. If the catalyst, used in the reaction, is homogeneous
the reactive volume of the distillation column can become diﬀicult to control accurately
[15]. However, if the catalyst is a heterogeneous solid, the reaction volume is instead fixed

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
to a specific part of the column. Usually it is located between a rectifying and stripping
sections to improve the column performance. The tray design for the reactive section
often resemble the tray design depicted in Figure 1.1 (b) or (c), where the packaging
material contains the active catalyst. Usually the column contains several feed locations,
placed strategically, to increase the eﬀiciency of the reactive section.
Despite reactive distillation having greatly improved the conventional operation for
equilibrium limited reactions, there is still room for improvement [13]. Kiss et. al. con-
ducted a comprehensive review of other PI technologies used in distillation to identify
potential combinations, which could significantly improve existing reactive distillations.
Among the possible combinations evaluated, periodic distillation emerged as an inter-
esting technology, due to a good control of residence time and energy savings in utility
consumption [13].
1.1.2
Periodic Distillation
Periodic distillation, also referred to as cyclic distillation, differentiate itself from other PI
technologies such as dividing wall columns, heat integration and membrane distillation by
not using the conventional liquid-vapor continuous flow, similar to the setup depicted in
Figure 1.1. Instead, it uses an alternative operating system that allows for separate phase
movement [14]. This is achieved by removing the down-comers from the conventional
sieve trays and cyclically turning the vapor flow on and off. During the vapor flow period
(VFP) the vapor moves through the perforated sieve trays and the liquid holdup, which
is restricted on the trays. Once the vapor flow is turned off, the liquid flow period (LFP)
begins, where it is now possible for the liquid phase to trickle down to the trays below.
Without the vapor flow restricting the liquid holdup, it begins to flow down from one
tray to the next. Afterwards, the VFP starts, and the cycle repeats.
The idea of periodic operation was introduced in the 1950s and 1960s by Cannon,
where extensive experimental and theoretical work, by McWirther, Shrodt and Gaska
showed a significant improvement in the operational energy eﬀiciency for sieve- and plate
distillation columns [21, 22, 23, 24]. The theoretical motivation for developing the al-
ternative operating method was to reduce back-mixing effects of the liquid. To achieve
this, it would require that the liquid holdups did not mix between trays, during the LFP.
Despite the promising results in laboratory scale experimentation, the method started
to suffer some practical diﬀiculties regarding pressure fluctuations and tray mixing at
larger scales [24]. Development continued until the 1990s, when academic and industrial
interest in the technology seized.
Interest was however reignited in 2011 when Maleta patented a new tray design for
periodic operation [25]. This design solved the issue of tray back-mixing by introduc-
ing a holdup sluice chamber that temporarily store the liquid holdup, and restrict the
phase movement to pass more than one tray during the LFP. With this new tray designs
a commercial implementation was realized in Ukraine, at the Lipnitsky Alcohol Plant,
where 30.000 liters per. day of a food grade ethanol mixture is stripped. According to
the plant operational data the separation eﬀiciency has been improved by 200-300% after
implementation of periodic operation [26]. An illustration of the tray function is depicted
6
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
in Figure 1.3.
Figure 1.3: Operating procedure for the Maleta type tray periodic operation.(a) Vapor flow
period, (b) Liquid flow period, (c) Start of new vapor flow period.
Figure 1.3 illustrates the ingenious design of the Maleta tray for cyclic operation
of columns. During the VLP depicted in fig. 1.3 (a), the vapor pressure, force a piston
located inside the sluice chamber to the top position. If the vapor flow is suﬀicient, it
can retain the liquid and instead bubble through the liquid holdup before moving into
the vapor holdup, and onto the tray above. This movement continues from each tray to
the next, for the duration of the VFP, or until the vapor reaches the top, where it is then
condensed [27].
Next, the liquid flow period is initiated, by turning the vapor flow off. The hydro-
static pressure of the liquid holdup moves the piston to the bottom position as depicted
in Figure 1.3 (b). This allows the liquid holdup to drain into the sluice chamber, while
simultaneously blocking it from draining into the succeeding tray. The liquid flow period
continues until the entire liquid holdup is drained from the tray into the sluice chamber
[27].
Once the liquid flow period ends, the cycle is repeated. As depicted in Figure 1.3 (c)
the vapor flow period will initially push the pistons back to the top of the sluice chamber
which allows for the liquid to drain onto the tray below. Once all liquid has drained
from the sluice chambers to the corresponding tray, the operating conditions depicted in
Figure 1.3 (a) is reached, and the cycle continues [27].
While the Maleta tray design solved the pressing issue of avoiding back-mixing,
there still occur pressure buildup in the reboiler due to the fluctuating vapor flow. Since
2011, a second alternative tray design, and operational doctrine, has been developed by
Toftegård et. al. in 2016 [28]. The tray design is called Cyclically Operated Perforated
Sheets (COPS) and function differently compared to the Maleta design, as depicted in
Figure 1.4.
The COPS trays employ a perforated sheet and structured packing, to allow for
continuous vapor flow while keeping the liquid holdup static on the respective trays. A
pneumatic system opens and closes the perforated trays in sequence to allow for the
7
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.2
liquid to flow from one tray to the tray directly below [14]. One after another, each tray
is drained until the top is reached. The cycle is then repeated.
Figure 1.4: Conceptual schematic of the operational cycle in periodic separation using COPS
trays. As the vapor flow rate is operated continuously the liquid holdup is retained until it is
drained sequentially.
In stripping experiments, using the COPS tray design, an increase in point eﬀiciency
by approximately 300%, and a factor 2 overall column eﬀiciency have been reported [28].
The increase in column performance was ultimately comparable to the results published
for the Maleta tray design. The sequential draining, combined with a continuous vapor
flow, has resulted in an increased pressure stability, especially for the operation of the
reboiler [28]. Currently, the simulation standard in industry is to assume a simultaneous
tray draining, mimicking the behavior expected for the Maleta tray setup [29]. Continued
research is conducted into whether the sequential draining by COPS trays are a significant
improvement compared to the Maleta trays, or if the two methods may differ in eﬀiciency
and practical applicability for specific chemical systems.
1.2
Combined Reactive- and Periodic Distillation
A recent qualitative study investigated the prospects of combining the novel reactive dis-
tillation process with other separation techniques, to further improve energy eﬀiciency.
Several factors are taken into account including the technological maturity, ease of imple-
mentation, range of applicability, capital- and utility savings. From the list of methods
investigated, the current front-runners are determined to be either the dividing wall
columns and cyclic separation operation [13].
Combining cyclic- and reactive distillation has been addressed previously by Maleta
et al. in 2011 [26], with the first mathematical model being developed by Pătruţ et al. in
2014 [1]. The models have become increasingly comprehensive for the individual technol-
ogy, as new iterations of the system interpretations include more complex dynamics. The
8
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.3
PROSYS research group has in the last 5 years diligently developed and expanded the
modeling and design methods for each individual PI methodology and combined system
behavior [30, 31, 32, 29]. This project therefore aims at contributing to the continued
development of the combined periodic and reactive distillation design methodologies.
Apart from tapping into the energy savings of implementing the periodic operation
for reactive distillation, the motivation also resides in improving the control of the resi-
dence time within the column. Since it has been recognized, that slow reactions require a
large residence time to reach equilibrium it would require a very large conventional reac-
tive distillation column to achieve a favorable operation. Instead, the combined reaction
and cyclic setup can be used to manipulate the VFP duration to allow for the reaction to
settle at the chemical equilibrium on each tray before the process is continued [18, 13].
However, there still remains uncertainty, as to how vapor- and liquid flow periods
should be selected for reactive distillations, operating periodically. The most recent design
algorithms still employ iterative schemes to determine period duration which are not only
computationally expensive but also slow, and can potentially diverge if initial guesses are
poor. The parameters are of particular interest when evaluating periodic implementation
for reactive system which are slow and require a large residence time to reach the chemical
equilibrium. In this aspect there is still debate, as to whether the most eﬀicient operation
is achieved by allowing all trays in the operating column to reach the chemical equilibrium,
or if the duration should be selected to intentionally avoid reaching the equilibrium.
1.3
Problem Statement
The objective of this thesis project is to determine, if the vapor flow duration, for period-
ically operated reactive distillation column can be arbitrarily selected, or are bound by
limitations. Using the most recent model iteration, it is attempted to compile a design
algorithm for improving the selection of the vapor flow duration for periodically operated
reactive distillation.
The scope of this master thesis project encompass a workload equivalent to 30 ECTS
points. The project will rely only on a theoretical study of mathematical system inter-
pretations and simulations. No physical experimentation is carried out as part of this
project work.
At the start of 2021, the spread of the novel corona virus, SARS-CoV-2, limited the
possibility of working in close physical proximity to supervisors and fellow students. It
has therefore been agreed upon, by supervisors and the author, to host weekly meetings,
every Tuesdays via. Zoom, to address any technical issues, or to discuss results, plans
etc. If questions arose during the week, it has been possible to contact at least one of the
supervisors and receive assistance via. e-mail.
To ensure a manageable and focused work-flow throughout the project, a project-
plan was generated. The work-load has been divided into distinct focus areas for which
designated time-slots have been allocated.
The project is planned to span 21 weeks

additional model iterations were implemented. Initially the case studies reported along-

Chapter 2
Literature Study
In the previous chapter, the concept of reactive- and periodic distillation are introduced
together with an outline for the thesis’ main objective. To conduct the desired study of
implementing periodic operation in reactive distillation columns, a more in depth techni-
cal review of the methodologies is required. The lack of access to experimental equipment
in the development of this project constrain the analysis to rely only on theoretical mod-
els. The literature study is therefore focused on developing trustworthy mathematical
models for conventional- and periodic reactive distillation columns, to be used in eval-
uating parameter effects on the reactive separation. Additionally, the literature study
will investigate the possible limitations of implementing reactive separations, and how
differences in conventional and periodic operation may shift the range of applicability.
Initially, the simplifying assumptions and thermodynamic models are introduced.
Later these are applied to derive the mathematical models used to simulate the vari-
ous reactive distillation columns. Once the thermodynamic and mathematical system
interpretations have been formulated, the feasibility of reactive distillation and periodic
operation is discussed. Whether or not a desired reaction and separation is feasible in
combined equipment, is determined based on case specific thermodynamics and opera-
tional limitations. Both aspects are discussed to identify possible improvements in the
range of applicability attributed by switching from conventional- to periodic operation of
the distillation column.
2.1
Assumptions
It is important to address that mathematical interpretations are only approximations
of natural phenomena. The lack of readily available experimental data ultimately pose
a challenge in determining the simulation model’s ability, to predict and mirror a real
process. This project therefore rely on conducting verification studies to establish trust to-
wards the derived system models, which is elaborated upon in Section 3.4. However, it is
important to keep the possible implications in mind when introducing simplifying assump-
tions, and how they might compromise the accuracy of the final derivation. To compare
the operation of conventional and period reactive distillation, two separate mathematical

2.2
Thermodynamic Models
The thermodynamic model encapsulates the system specific thermo-physical relationships
which includes determination of the vapor-liquid equilibrium and extents of chemical
reactions. While the thermo-physical behavior of any chemical system varies depending
on the species included, they are bound by the same selection of models used to determine
relevant properties. In the subsections below, these common models used to compute
system properties are presented, and justified by referencing assumptions introduced in
table 2.1.
2.2.1
Phase Equilibrium
The phase equilibrium is perhaps the most important thermodynamic property to accu-
rately determine when modeling any distillation processes, since the separation technique
employs a difference in vapor and liquid composition, to purify the most volatile species.
Generally the distribution of any species in the vapor and liquid phase are dependent
on the Gibbs free energy. It is possible to calculate a distribution factor K, named the
equilibrium constant, by minimizing the Gibbs free energy associated with a change in
composition of the two phases [9]. There exist many options for calculating the equilib-
rium constant, and a conscious decision is required, to select a model which satisfy the
specific application.
In this thesis, moderate/high accuracy for a wide range of chemical systems are
preferred. Additionally, a relatively low complexity is favored to reduce the computational
strain of running extensive simulations. Therefore, this report adapts the practices used
12
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.2
in the majority of previous studies, by implementing the modified Raoults law to describe
the vapor-liquid-equilibrium (VLE) [29, 33, 32].
yi,j = γi,jP sat
i,j
Pn
· xi,j = K · xi,j
(2.1)
The VLE model depicted in eq. (2.1) is still considered somewhat simple, since it
assumes that the vapor phase can be accurately models as an ideal gas. However, the VLE
model does improve upon the regular Raoult’s law model, by including a liquid activity
coeﬀicient, γ, which allows the VLE computation to include non-ideal liquid behavior.
This is necessary, to capture the presence of azeotropes in the chemical systems [9]. There
are several different activity models which can be used to determine γ. These models
all require component-specific parameters, to arbitrarily compute the liquid activities.
Therefore, has the selection of liquid activity models, used in this project, depended on the
available parameters for the specific case systems. In total, the NRTL and Wilson models
are employed and have been presented in the Chapter A, together with the coeﬀicients
used in the relevant case studies.
Introducing assumption (2) Tray phase equilibrium removes the resistance to inter-
phase mass transfer between the liquid and vapor phase. Therefore, this thesis report
does not include a rate-based mass transfer model to describe possible mass-transfer
limited distillation systems.
Instead the thesis report only address chemical systems
which are kinetically controlled. This can be interpreted as an instantaneous adjustment
of the vapor phase composition, to accommodate the possible changes in the liquid mole
fractions, due to a chemical reaction taking place. In reality, the mass transfer rate is
heavily dependent on the operating conditions and column internals, which can shift the
working regime by changing the mass-transfer constant kLa [16, 20].
2.2.2
Chemical Reactions
The type of chemical reaction which have warranted most attention, regarding implemen-
tation into reactive separation equipment, are equilibrium limited reactions [16, 1, 32].
These types of chemical systems are challenged by low pass-through conversion rates in
conventional reactors, primarily due to the reaction equilibrium being the limiting factor.
If the pass-through conversion is low, the exiting flows from the reactor will consist
of a large fraction of unreacted reactants. In the downstream processes these reactants
must be separated from the product flow, and subsequently recycled, in order for the
chemical facility to reach overall reactant conversion goals. A large recycle volume also
increase the energy expenses consumed during separation. Improving the pass-through
conversion of the process, could greatly improve the energy eﬀiciency overall .
A mixture which undergoes equilibrium reactions, consists of a set of distinct forward-
and backwards reactions expressions, which produce and consume the product specie re-
spectively. A chemical equilibrium composition is reached, once the rate of both reactions
are equalized. Real chemical systems often contains several intermediary- and side reac-
13
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.2
tion, described by distinct rate expressions and associated intermediary species. However,
for the sake of simplicity, only a forward and backward rate expressions are used to illus-
trate the interpretation of chemical equilibrium system going forward.

Chemical equilibrium reactions can be modeled mathematically by employing two
different approaches. One approach, is to include the time dependent reaction kinetics,
incorporating the rate of consumption and production, into the mass- and energy balance
equations.
The second approach is to assume that each tray immediately reach the
equilibrium liquid concentration, and use this knowledge to determine the associated
tray liquid holdup. The simplest of the two approaches, is to assume that the chemical
equilibrium is reached immediately on each tray [17]. This assumption implies that the
chemical reactions are very fast, which can be a justifiable assumption for a selection of
different chemical systems.
The chemical equilibrium is defined as the point, where the change in Gibbs free
energy, of either the forward- or backwards reaction, reaches zero.

2.2.3
Azeotropes and Reactive Azeotropes
An azeotrope is a phenomenon which sometimes occur in a distillation process, and it
constitutes a thermodynamic limit on the possible separation. Azeotropes are mixture
dependent, and they appear when the relative volatility of species in the mixture ap-
proach αij →1. This translates to the saturated composition of the vapor and liquid
phase becoming identical, which in turn removes the possibility of achieving further pu-
rification by evaporating the most volatile component. An azeotrope can be categorized
as either a high-boiling- or low-boiling type azeotrope, depicted in Figure 2.1 (A) and
(B) respectively. The classification denotes whether the azeotrope mixture obtains a sat-
uration temperature above or below the pure species saturation temperatures. A famous
example of a binary azeotrope mixture is water and ethanol, which at ambient pressure
form a low-boiling-point azeotrope at a composition of approximately 89 mol% ethanol
[35].
Several PI strategies exist, which can overcome the azeotrope point, including extrac-
tive distillation, pressure swing strategies or liquid-liquid extractions [9]. Alternatively,
the introduction of a chemical reaction can potentially alter the composition of the mix-
ture to mitigate the azeotrope point as depicted in Figure 2.1 [36, 37].
If given the composition, pressure and temperature at the azeotrope point, it is
possible to evaluate the chemical equilibrium reaction at the azeotrope conditions. If
the equilibrium reaction favors the reactant side, or if the equilibrium constant is unity,
it is not thermodynamically possible to overcome the azeotrope point, solely from im-
plementing the chemical reaction. If instead the product side is favored, it is possible
to employ the reaction to overcome the azeotrope point after which the rectifying- or
stripping section of the column can readily purify the product [15, 16, 18].
15
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.2
Figure 2.1: Binary Txy-diagramt for a hypothetical binary mixture of compound A and B.
(A) The mixture of the two components form a high-boiling point azeotrope. (B) The mixture
form a low-boiling point azeotrope. In (C) and (D) are a chemical equilibrium reaction included.
For the selection of equilibrium constant in (C) the mixture does not form an azeotrope. In (D)
the selection of the equilibrium does form a reactive low-boiling point azeotrope.
The cases presented in Figure 2.1 only address binary azeotropes, which encompass
mixtures of only two components. However, it is also possible for high- and low-boiling-
point azeotropes to form for ternary and quaternary mixtures containing three and four
components.
It is common to employ residue curve maps (RCM) when depicting the change in
liquid composition for mixtures of more than two components [9]. This method consists of
evaluating the mixture composition in a batch tank as content is evaporated. Commonly,

2.2.4
Enthalpy
The change in enthalpy is a widely employed method to quantify the change in stored
energy within the system, at a given temperature and pressure. Enthalpy expands on
the internal energy term by including heat and work as sources of stored energy [34].
Determining the stored energy of input and output streams can be used to set up an
energy balance around each tray in a distillation column, where the heat of reaction
effects can be included [9, 16].
By including the energy balance the required condenser and reboiler heat duties,
and include heat of reaction in the energy balance can be computed. Ultimately the heat
effect’s impact on the internal flow magnitudes can be assessed which has shown to affect
the results greatly [40]. Assuming, that pressure fluctuations are negligible the change
in enthalpy of the liquid and vapor phases are calculated as the temperature integral
of the specific heat capacity Cp,i. It is assumed that the specific heat capacity of the
liquid and vapor phase are independent of temperature and pressure, thus making it a
constant. This greatly simplify the calculation of liquid and vapor phase enthalpy as
seen in eq. (2.15a) and eq. (2.15b). Specification of a reference state is necessary since
enthalpy is a state function, evaluating a change in stored energy. The standard ambient
temperature and pressure (SATP) conditions at Pref = 1 [atm] and Tref = 298.15 [K]
are selected as the reference point with a numeric enthalpy of h0 = 0. This denotes the
standard state which is commonly used in conventional reporting of thermodynamic data
[34].

investigated in this thesis project.

2.3
Conventional Reactive Distillation
In the previous section, the interpretation of relevant thermo-physical models are pre-
sented. Using these interpretations together with the assumptions listed in Table 2.1,
the next step is to develop mathematical expressions which describe the dynamics of
conventional- and periodic reactive distillation. Of these two methods, it is the conven-
tional operation which is currently the most mature, where detailed derivations of both
equilibrium phase- and rate-based tray interpretations have existed since the late 1990s
and early 2000s [41, 42, 16].
The standard approach to constructing mathematical models that govern the dy-
namics of any conventional distillation process, is to initially formulate an interpretation
of the individual distillation trays. Once a mass- and energy balance has been derived
for individual distillation trays, they are put into a sequence to imitate the dynamics of
a multi-tray distillation column. In this thesis project, are the models used to simulate
conventional reactive trays derived from an expanded interpretation of regular conven-
tional distillation columns as depicted in Figure 1.1. A more detailed and generalized
visualization of the tray balance is presented in Figure 2.2 which includes a reaction rate
term.
19
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.3
Figure 2.2: A conceptual representation of the system interpretations used to derive the mass-
and energy balance for a conventional stage for periodic reactive distillation.
By applying assumptions (2) Tray phase equilibrium and (7) Perfect tray mixing it
is assumed that there is no resistance to inter-phase mass transfer, and no concentration
gradient in the liquid hold-up on each tray. It is then possible to employ the mass- and
energy balance equations which govern equilibrium trays in regular distillation, commonly
referred to as the Material balance, Equilibrium relations, Summation equations and
Heat balances (MESH) equations [9][39].
An extension is however required since the
original formulation does not contain a reaction rate term. However, these equations are
easily modified by adding arbitrary stoichiometric coeﬀicients, ν, and rates of reaction,
r, to the mass balance, and simultaneously adding heat of reaction, ∆rH, to the energy
balance. Combined with implementation of assumptions (4) No heat of mixing and (5)
Negligible heat loss the energy balance requires no additional modification. Thus the
new formulation of the MESH equations which can accommodate reactions are printed
in eq. (2.20) [41].
Applying assumption (1) Vapor holdup negligible, mass- and energy balances are only
set up for the liquid molar holdup. This implies that the vapor residence time approach
zero, which translates to a relatively large vapor flow compared to vapor volume between
20
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.3
trays. In total the number of model equations is then reduced by an order of two.

In more recent publications, the Aspen plus RADFRAC simulator is employed to
compute the steady state operation of equilibrium trays [43, 44, 45]. However, the mass
transfer resistance can significantly affect the operational requirements of a reactive dis-
tillation column, to a degree, where the weeping and flooding requirements produce an
infeasible construction [42]. Despite non-equilibrium phase modeling of reactive separa-
tions are important prior to implementation in industrial applications [42, 16], preliminary
studies are conducted, using the simpler equilibrium tray formulation [16, 41].
The main focus of this thesis project is to investigate parameter effects in periodic
reactive separation, where non-equilibrium phase tray models have not yet been derived
and verified [29]. It is therefore unnecessary, to increase the complexity of the conventional
reactive separation model, since it serves as a comparative study, and should contain
a similar degree of complexity to better compare results between the two operational
methods.
2.4
Periodic Reactive Distillation
The mathematical model for periodic reactive distillation is derived, using the same ap-
proach employed for the conventional operation. Namely, constructing a mass- and en-
ergy balance around a single periodic tray, and compiling a sequence of connected tray
balances to capture the dynamics of the column. The theoretical models for periodic
distillation has existed since the early 1960’s [21] and has continued to be improved upon
until the most recent published interpretation by Rasmussen et. al. in 2020 [29].
The oscillating nature of the interrupted vapor flow operation, has spawned a stan-
dard approach to modeling the mass- and energy balance, which consist of separating the
dynamics into two distinct mathematical expressions, capturing the vapor- and liquid
22
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.4
flow period respectively. Figure 2.3 illustrates distinct mathematical interpretations of
the two operational methods for a periodically operated separation column [27, 29, 46,
21].
Only recently has the first realization of a mathematical model that combined re-
action and periodic separation been published by Pătruţ et. al. in 2014 [1]. This is
currently the standard modeling approach for periodic reactive columns. Andersen et.
al. [33] expanded the nonreactive periodic separation model in 2018 by included a more
generalized energy balance in his iteration. This was refined and generalized further in
the most recent mathematical model for periodic separation presented by Rasmussen et.
al.
in 2020 [29].
However, in neither of the models presented by Andersen [33] and
Rasmussen [29] have the effects of chemical reactions been included. While the model
by Rasumussen does include a general heat term, allowing for the heat of reaction to be
added into the energy balance, it has not been derived with inclusion of reactive systems
in mind [29].
Figure 2.3: A conceptual representation of the system interpretations used to derive the mass-
and energy balance for an equilibrium stage for periodic reactive distillation (left) vapor flow
period and (right) liquid flow period.
Therefore, the most recently published model for reactive and periodic distillation
available in literature is still provided by Pătruţ [1]. It has been speculated, if this project
should include the derivation and implementation of an extended version of the Andersen
23
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.4
[33] and Rasmussen [29] periodic operation model. However, due to subsequent issues
of verifying the derived model against a limited amount of accessible experimental data,
and the main objective being situated elsewhere, this was abandoned. Since Pătruţ [1]
derived his model by employing a simplified energy balance to calculate the vapor flow as
a function of time, the conventional and periodic models are not derived from an identical
initial interpretation, which is important to keep in mind when comparing results in a
later chapter [1, 29].
From Table 2.1 many of the same assumptions employed in deriving the set of equa-
tions describing the conventional reactive operation. Additionally, the introduction of
(3) No back-mixing results in the separation of the dynamics into a vapor flow period
and liquid flow period as depicted in Figure 2.3. Assumption (6) Instantaneous liquid
displacement reduces the liquid flow mass- and energy balance to a discrete event where
the content of each tray is computed without time dependence [21, 1, 29].
As depicted in Figure 2.3 the dynamics of a periodic operation vary depending on
the specific period in the operational cycle.
Therefore, two sets of mass- and energy
balances are required to capture the dynamics of the liquid holdup. Due to the lack of a
liquid counter-current flow during the vapor flow period, each tray can be interpreted as
a series of batch reactors [9]. From the assumption of instantaneous liquid displacement
the liquid holdup of each tray is simply shifted down one tray with optional feed and
liquid side draw accounted for.

An important observation, which should be addressed, is the occurrence of what
appears to be a typographical error in the published paper by Pătruţ[1]. In the tray in-
dexing of the vapor flow rate eq. (2.28), he has employed ∆vapHj instead of ∆vapHj+1 to
compute the energy supplied from the tray below. Upon discussing this finding with su-
pervisors and re-evaluating the energy balance presented in eq. (2.26), this was concluded
to be an unconscious typographical error, which would be averted when implementing
the simulation model in this project. It is important to keep this typographical error in
mind, as it will return when discussing model verification in section 3.4.
The lack of an explicit energy balance used in deriving the model for periodic reac-
tive separation, must also be addressed when comparing the effects of conventional and
periodic operation. Consequently, the models are not derived at a completely identical
level of complexity which might affect the final results.

method to compare the effects of the design decision, it is possible to compare the vapor
flow velocities of the periodic model by Pătruţ and Rasmussen and observe the deviation
[12, 29].
Since the main objective of this project is to establish general correlations between
the overall system dynamics and operational parameter. A lower level of complexity will
ultimately affect the numerical results but should hopefully not change the overall trends
observed.

It is observed that while the reflux- and boil-up ratio appear similar to the formula-
tion printed for the conventional separation, the vapor flow period duration is included
in eq. (2.32). Since both the liquid reflux and liquid distillate are reported in units per
cycle, due to the discrete nature of the liquid flow model, the ratio is readily determined.
However, the vapor flow from the reboiler is reported in units per time, due to the con-
tinuous nature of the vapor flow model. Assuming that the vapor flow magnitude from
the reboiler is distributed equally throughout the vapor flow period, the total content
evaporated during one cycle is readily computed [1].
Recalling the importance of reflux- and boil-up ratio in achieving desired purity and
recovery from conventional distillation, it is clear from the formulation above that the
vapor flow period can strongly affect the eﬀiciency of the operation, directly affecting the
purity and recovery of the distillate and bottoms flows. In a later section it becomes clear
that the selection of vapor flow period duration can have multiple motivations regarding,
not only, the separation performance, but also extent of reaction.

2.5
Feasibility
Reactive distillation is, as previously mentioned, not a universally applicable PI technol-
ogy and strongly depend on the thermo-physical properties of specific chemical systems
[15].
Depicted in Figure 2.4 are illustrated the potentially narrow operating window,
where a temperature and pressure region allow for both separation and reaction to occur
within the equipment specification.
Figure 2.4: Depiction of the narrow operating window available to perform reactive distillation
[13].
Early experiments by Barbosa and Doherty evaluated residue curve maps (RCM)
of reactive mixtures and assessed, if implementing an equilibrium reaction could over-
come non-reactive azeotropes [17]. They demonstrated a clear success, which they later
expanded from a simple batch formulation to a multi-component reactive column with
one or two feeds [37, 36]. In these early distillation column formulation, they presented
strategies for determining a minimum-reflux ratios, which was required to achieve a de-
sired separation that could bypass non-reactive azeotropes [37, 36].
Later the assumption that the chemical reaction always reach equilibrium was re-
laxed. Here Venimadhaven et. al. [38] published his findings in 1994, which confirmed
that kinetic effects play a significant role in the feasibility of implementing reactive sep-
aration. His research was conducted by evaluating residue curve maps in simple reactive
27
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.5
batch distillation. He compared the RCM for different Damköhler numbers which mea-
sure the ratio of liquid residence time to rates of reaction [38]. In 1998 Okasinski and
Doherty expanded the research to account for kinetic effects when designing staged reac-
tive distillation columns [47]. Both studies found that if a chemical reaction is suﬀiciently
slow, the residence time on each reactive tray can impact the extent of conversion to a
point, where the non-reactive azeotrope no longer can be bypassed. To quantify the rela-
tive residence time of species on each tray to the rate of reaction, both papers introduce
the dimensionless Damköhler number [38, 47]. Okasinski and Doherty [47] are also the
first to capture the heat of reaction effects on the required number of separation trays.
Figure 2.5: A framework for a technical evaluation of reactive distillation processes (Reprint
from Shah 2012 [20])
Recently extensive studies have been conducted by Shah et al. in 2012 [20] and
Muthia et al. in 2020 [48] to construct effective screening strategies acting as a preliminary
evaluation of the feasibility of implementing reactive separation on arbitrary chemical
processes [20, 48]. While the RCM strategy is still considered invaluable in evaluating
the prospect of implementing reactive distillation, the strategies by Shah and Muthia
primarily consist of evaluating species’ relative volatility and the chemical equilibrium
constant [41, 16, 20, 48]. The main motivation behind the technical framework presented
by Shah [20] depicted in Figure 2.5, is to reduce the efforts required to screen the feasibility
of implementing reactive separation for any given chemical system, without immediately
resorting to extensive simulations and design algorithms. The issue remains that some
28
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.5
systems are thermodynamically feasible but may be impractical to implement. This could
consists of a low relative volatility between species which would require a very large
column, or potential formation of reactive azeotropes which would constrain distillate
and bottoms purity[20, 16, 49].
The Damköhler number is an important factor in designating feasible designs in both
evaluation strategies, and encompass both column configuration and operating condition.
2.5.1
Damköhler Number
The Damköhler number represents the ratio between liquid residence time and rate of
reaction. It encapsulates whether a molecule entering a tray is more likely to pass through
the tray unchanged or undergo reaction. Depending on the interpretation of the reactive
equipment and chemical system, the residence time of a liquid may change, and the rate
of reaction may depend on the holdup size. An example is the formulation presented by
Venimadhavan in eq. (2.33) for a reactive batch system [38].
Da = H0
V kf
(2.33)
Here Ho is the liquid hold-up, V is the vapor flow rate and kf is a pseudo first order
rate constant. If the Damköhler number is small (Da ≤0.1) the dominating aspect is
the phase equilibrium, and the residue curve map will represent that of a non-reactive
separation. If the Damköhler number is large it indicates a fast reaction, and the chemical
equilibrium dominates the system dynamics, resulting in a residue curve map resembling
that of an instantaneous chemical equilibrium [16, 38].
For a sequence of reacting trays, Okasinski and Doherty [47] proposed an alternative
formulation, which has been employed in case studies conducted by Huss et. al. and
Chen et. al. in the early 2000’s [47, 40, 39].
Da = HT
R
F kf
(2.34)
The new formulation is very similar to eq. (2.33) with the slight alteration of switch-
ing the vapor flow rate with the feed rate F, and using the total reactive liquid hold-up
inside the column [40, 39].
While the specific formulations differ for the two system interpretations they repre-
sent the same dimensionless measurement. If the reaction is very fast i.e. kf →∞the
assumption of reaching an instantaneous equilibrium is valid. This will in turn result in
Da →∞given an arbitrary selection of holdup and vapor flow rate. If instead the reac-
tion is very slow converging to non-reactivity, the Damköhler number becomes Da →0.
Both cases represent the extreme boundaries of non-reactive system and instantaneous
equilibrium. Somewhere in-between is a critical value, where Da < Dacrit the desired
29
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.5
separation is not reached, whether that be a desired degree of conversion or passing a
non-reactive azeotrope point [47, 38].
Increasing the Damköhler number in conventional operation would require an in-
crease in the column size, as indicated by eq. (2.34). Kiss et. al expressed in 2018 [13]
that periodic operation could solve this problem, since the liquid residence time is con-
trolled via the vapor flow duration tV FP, thus solving the issue for slow chemical reactions.
In a conventional reactive separation column, the operation will eventually converge to
a steady state, where internal flows and holdups are independent of time. In this case
the Damköhler number of each individual tray, and the column as a whole, will as well
converge to constant values. In periodic operation only a pseudo steady state is reached
where the Damköhler number for a periodic tray is expected to change during the time-
span of the vapor flow period, as content is evaporated depicted in Figure 2.6. Thus,
the time averaged value will change depending on the selection of the vapor flow period
duration. The idea, is therefore to directly control the residence time of liquid holdup on
each tray, to allow for a suﬀicient extent of reaction to take place, potentially elevating
previously disregarded slow reaction to a feasible implementation in reactive separation
columns.
Figure 2.6: Damköhler number given identical hold-up and vapor flow rate for (left) conven-
tional and (right) periodic operation of a reactive distillation.
The introduction of periodic operation will not alter the thermo-physical character-
istics of the chemical system and therefore non-reactive and reactive azeotropes may still
pose serious problems.
The introduction of periodic operation instead aims at allow-
ing for previously discarded chemical systems such as Methyl Acetate Hydrolysis to be
reevaluated and potentially realizable [20, 12].
30
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE STUDY
2.5
2.5.2
Weeping & Tray Liquid Holdup
Once the separation problem has been solved, and the operational parameters which
satisfy the top and bottom purity, and recovery has been determined, the next step is
compute the size of the column. In conventional distillation the size of column diameter
is determined by evaluating a term called flooding. This refers to a phenomena where the
vapor velocity is so large, it is able to carry liquid droplets backwards up the column. This
greatly reduces the operational eﬀiciency, and can lead to potentially dangerous situations
[9]. By increasing the cross-sectional area of the column, and increasing the perforated
fraction of the tray, the vapor velocity can be reduced to avoid this phenomena. This is
not possible in Maleta trays due to the incorporation of a retention chamber between the
stages [26]. Instead, another phenomena known as weeping can occur.
Weeping occur, if the vapor velocity drops below a threshold dictated by the liquid
holdup on each tray. At this point it is not possible for the vapor flow to retain the
liquid holdup on its tray, resulting in liquid leaking to trays below. Since no unique
correlation is available which describe the hydrodynamic effect for a Maleta type tray,
the Eduljee correlation for sieve trays, printed in eq. (2.35), are assumed applicable
[50, 32]. These equations estimate the minimum vapor velocity, by introducing a height
dependent constant used together with a hole diameter and vapor density to compute the
required force to restrain the liquid on a given tray. From a re-computation of the Eduljee
correlation presented by Thakore and Bhatt [51] it has been possible for Nielsen [50] to
fit sigmoidal curve allowing for an arbitrary determination of the correlated parameter,
given the liquid height on each tray [50].
Since flooding has been deemed virtually impossible on the Maleta trays [26], one
could incorrectly assume, there is no upper practical limit for the specification of the
vapor flow rate and vapor velocity. However, as the periodic operation partly resembles
a series of batch reactors, the total liquid holdup gradually decrease over the duration of
the vapor flow period. As the vapor flow rate increase the liquid downcome decrease. At
some point the liquid holdup reaching the bottom is less, than the amount evaporated
in the vapor flow period. The liquid holdup in the reboiler will then decrease gradually
until it empties, at which point no vapor can be generated and the entire operation breaks
down.
The operation of both conventional and periodic distillation is constrained by vapor
flow requirements that exceed the weeping flow limit.
These velocities are functions
of both operation and column internal design. The specific details of internal design is
disregarded in this project, but are nonetheless important at later stages in column design,
when evaluating assumptions of perfect tray mixing and mass transfer limitations. For
the purpose of this project, the column diameter dcol and bubble-cap tray, shieve- and

==PAGE:44==

In eq. (2.35) are presented the calculation procedure for determining the minimum

A thorough evaluation of the Maleta patent [25] has been conducted by Nielsen [50]
whom estimated the approximate hole diameter, and reported this as a rectangular 3/15
[mm] slits of which 12 were placed on each piston. The piston density on a common tray
is estimated at approximately 100 pistons per. [m2][50]. In total Nielsen approximated
the relative hole area of a common Maleta tray to be 6% with a circular hole diameter of
7 −8 [mm] [50]. A liquid holdup height of 250 [mm] seemed to be a reasonable limit for
the purpose of this thesis which has also been used as an upper limit in previous work
[32].
It is assumed that the sizing of the individual pistons are inflexible. The only method
available to design the Maleta trays, are to change the area frequency of pistons, or to
change the total column diameter. This is implemented as a hole fraction, α, and column
diameter, dcol.
