2.6
2.6
Concluding Remarks
In this chapter the system interpretation of a conventional- and periodic reactive distilla-
tion unit has been translated into mathematical expression that can perform quantitative
simulations. While the models are derived by employing the same simplifying assump-
tions printed in Table 2.1, the periodic model has employed a simplified version of the
energy balance. This is important to keep in mind when comparing the simulation results
for the conventional and periodic operation.
The feasibility of implementing reactive separations has been discussed and found
to be constrained by a narrow operating field which become diﬀicult to reach when the
chemical reactions are slow. The requirement for increased liquid residence time are rele-
vant issues which could potentially be solved by the implementation of periodic operation.
Weeping and flooding are common operational constrains in the design of conventional
distillation columns. These are primarily used to determine the column diameter and in-
ternal tray design. Issues for weeping are also found in periodic operation, which puts a
lower limit on the reboiler duty. It is assumed that flooding is not possible [26]. However,
it is possible to select a vapor flow rate from the reboiler in periodic operation which
result in gradual emptying of the reboiler which eventually lead to operational failure.
Mapping methods are presented, which evaluate the feasibility of implementing arbi-
trary chemical systems in conventional reactive distillation columns, without employing
complex and comprehensive simulation studies [20, 49, 48]. The dimensionless Damköhler
number is employed to evaluate if the operational configuration is technically or econom-
ically feasible. The Damköhler number describes the ratio between rate of reaction and
average residence time. This parameter has previously been used as a design parame-
ter for conventional reactive distillation to determine operational configurations which
reduce utility consumption while ensuring desired overall column operation [40, 39].
33
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3
Model Implementation
The system interpretations of a conventional and periodic reactive column are presented in
section 2.3 and section 2.4. From these interpretations, a set of mathematical expressions
are subsequently derived, which capture the dynamics of a reactive column operating
either conventionally or periodically. To readily utilize any mathematical models and
design algorithms in simulation based analysis, the mathematical expressions must be
implemented in appropriate software.
This chapter elaborates on the technical implementation of ODEs and non-linear
expressions into MATLAB© R2020b syntax. Including, a detailed description of the nu-
merical solver strategies governed by ’Fixed-Point Iteration’, and the periodic separation
design method of ’Backwards-Integration’ [46].
The final section of this chapter is dedicated to verifying the implementation of the
simulation models. After discussing the concepts of validation and verification, three
case studies are introduced. These include; methyl acetate, dimethyl ether (DME) and
methyl tert-buthyl ether (MTBE) synthesis. All case studies have previously been inves-
tigated in literature and simulation results from previous studies are readily available. As
part of the verification procedure, these published results are replicated using the model
implementation performed in this thesis project.
3.1
Back-Integration
To obtain steady state tray holdups for reactive and non-reactive conventional distillation,
the MESH equations can be readily solved using a fixed-point non-linear solver. Periodic
operation however, never reach a final steady state. Instead, it converges to a repeating
oscillating pattern which is denoted the pseudo steady-state. In early developments, the
pseudo-steady state liquid holdup is determined by conducting a dynamic simulation
of the complete model from an initial tray holdup estimate. The dynamic simulation
continued until the periodic steady state has been reached [52]. This required an extensive
computational time to evaluate different design setups and operating conditions.
34
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.1
Toftegård and Jørgensen introduced in 1988 the backwards-integration methodology
as an alternative design algorithms for computing the required number of separation
trays in a periodic distillation unit. This ingenious approach allowed for the step-wise
construction of periodic columns, when only the bottom liquid holdup is specified. The
algorithm works by integrating the model equations backwards in time until the start of
the VFP. At this point the liquid tray holdup must be identical to the liquid holdup at the
end of the LFP. Now, the liquid flow model is integrated backwards in time will estimate
the liquid holdup on the tray above at the end of the VFP. With the added knowledge of
the next tray holdup, the scheme could be repeated until a desired separation quality is
reached [46]. Despite, that the method is only applicable for non-reactive separation, it
still has relevance in reactive separation design. For example, Pătruţ [1] used backwards-
integration to compute the number of required stripper and rectifying trays in his reactive
column. He then used an assumption of constant rate of reaction to estimate the required
number of reactive trays [1]. Nielsen [32] did however illustrate in 2018 that Pătruţ [1]
greatly overestimate the number of required reactive trays by relying on constant rate
of reaction [32]. However, this approach does allow for a decent initial guess on column
designs that satisfy the desired reaction and separation, which can be improved afterwards
by iteration.
Figure 3.1: Illustration of the backwards-integration concept, which gradually determine the
pseudo steady state liquid molar holdup on trays in a periodic separation, starting at the reboiler
and terminating at the top.
The underlying concept for the algorithm is illustrated in Figure 3.1. The design
constraints for the outlet composition xB, and liquid molar holdup of the reboiler M (V )
NT , at
the end of the VFP, are treated as initial values in the backwards-integration algorithm.
The mass balance equation for the reboiler is then integrated backwards in time from
t = [tvap; 0] using the aforementioned initial condition. Once the backwards-integration
35
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.2
terminates, the molar holdup in the reboiler has reverted to the start of the VFP. It is
known that this holdup equals the molar holdup, at the end of the LFP. By backwards
integrating the LFP balance equations, using the result of the VFP backwards-integration
as a new initial condition, the liquid molar holdup at the tray above can be determined.
Now, the liquid molar holdup of the bottom two trays are known, at the end of the VFP.
Thus, the solver is expanded to include the models for both trays, and the backwards
integration process is repeated until the molar holdup throughout the column has been
determined [46, 14].
Pătruţ [1] employed the method in his DME design, to determine the number of
stripping trays required to reach a non-reactive azeotrope. After modeling the desired
conversion, the rectifying section is calculated using the results from the last reactive
tray as initial condition and repeating the algorithm until a desire distillate composition
has been reached [1]. To remain consistent, when presenting simulation and design data
for periodically operated columns, the tray holdup at the end of the VFP is used as
the reference timestamp to compare simulations operating at pseudo steady-state [32, 1].
This point in the pseudo steady-state cycle is selected, since the composition at the end
of the VFP equals the final composition of the distillate and bottoms leaving the column
in the LFP.
3.2
Fixed-Point Iteration
Solving large systems of nonlinear equations is a delicate problem to address, where the
algorithms can easily diverge or produce practically infeasible solutions. The first step
in solving such systems is to ensured that the number of nonlinear equations match
the degrees of freedom.
Otherwise, it is impossible to find a converged and explicit
solution. The fixed-point algorithm approach is used in this project to solve the steady
state operational conditions for conventional reactive distillation. Often these algorithms
employ a Newton-Raphson gradient descent approach to iteratively update the list of
variables by evaluating the Jacobian of the equation system, as illustrated in eq. (3.1)[47]:
∆X = −
[
∂F
∂X
]−1
· F
(3.1a)
Xk+1 = Xk + t∆Xk
(3.1b)
In eq. (3.1) the bold font letters indicate vectors, where X denote unknown variables
solved for F denoting the non-linear equations.
The parameter t is a weight, which
can be manipulated as the solution converge, to avoid undesired fluctuations around the
minimum. Usually, the implemented solvers in MATLAB© determine this parameter at
each iteration to avoid the gradient becoming too steep. A visual representation of the
fixed point algorithm is depicted in Figure 3.2
An alternative to using fixed point algorithms, are employing MINLP operations
36
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.2
which minimizes an objective functions and serves as an optimization approach to design
of reactive distillation columns. However, this requires extremely heavy computation [44].
For the purposes of this project, fixed-point is deemed an acceptable solving scheme.
Figure 3.2: Simplified overview of (left) the iterative fixed-point algorithms employed to solve
steady state conventional distillation, and (right) the dynamic simulation of periodic operation.
The MATLAB© R2020b toolbox include robust nonlinear solvers which are employed
to solve the large set of nonlinear equations, required to obtain the steady state opera-
tion for a conventional reactive distillation column. A decent initial guess is however
mandatory to guarantee that the solvers converge to a finite and real solution.
Two solvers are used in this thesis project namely fzero and lsqnonlin.
fzero is
a very fast, fixed-point solver, which only requires a function handle and initial guess
to determine a solution x which returns f(x) = 0.
Unfortunately, the function can
only handle single equation systems, which makes it unfit to solve the modified MESH
equations for an entire distillation column. However, it is used in the periodic model
to compute the saturation temperature on each tray. The function, lsqnonlin, is used
instead to solve the modified MESH equations. This algorithm requires a function handle
referring to an m-file containing the modified MESH equations printed in eq. (2.20),
and an initial guess. The great benefit of selecting this particular solver, compared to
more common alternatives such as fsolve, is that lsqnonlin allows for optional boundary
37
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.3
specification. This allows the user to specify the feasible region which immediately reduce
the likelihood of the converged solution settling around an infeasible minimum.
The
source code for the solver setup can be found printed in Appendix Chapter B.
Due to the oscillating operation of the periodic column, it is not possible to employ
the non-linear solvers to compute a steady state. Instead, a series of transient simulation
are conducted until the liquid molar holdup at the end of a LFP equals the liquid molar
holdup at the start of the VFP. This algorithm is printed on Figure 3.2 next to the fixed
point iterative scheme.
3.3
Architecture
The developed source-code used to conduct the analysis presented in this report, has
been attached in Chapter B. Initially, the vision has been to implement a very generalized
thermodynamic- and system model, assisted by many single-operation functions requiring
few inputs. This is developed to increase the flexibility of the model and potentially allow
for a more thorough investigation of the dynamic effects of implementing side-draws,
manipulating the vapor/liquid fraction of the feeds, and including multiple reactions.
Ultimately, many of the generalized attributes are not employed in the final analysis, due
to the strict project time constraint.
The thermodynamic model consists of several property functions used to determine
e.g. saturated temperatures, heats of vaporization and molar densities. These functions
are extracted from the Aspen Plus V11 database together with the species unique property
constants. Static scripts have been implemented for each chemical system evaluated in
this project. These scripts contain all constants and case specific parameters used in the
thermodynamic model. This decision has been made, to avoid user error when loading
the mixture properties to simulation scripts. To ensure that the static functions contains
updated thermodynamic property values, the author accessed Aspen Plus V11 databases
on the 2nd of June 2021 and copied the updated parameter data to the relevant tables
printed for pure substances and binary interactions in Chapter A.
The distillation columns are more variable in nature. Here object oriented program-
ming is used to construct a class, which could store essential column properties in a
standardized format, independent of operational method and chemical system. This is
implemented to ensure that the investigation of the columns would be less prone to
user error when working with several columns simultaneously, and to standardize the
construction of parameter vectors used in simulation scripts.
MATLAB© R2020b contains several predefined numerical integrators capable of solv-
ing ordinary differential equations (ODE) and systems of ODEs. Previous work conducted
by Nielsen in 2018 [32] employed the non-stiff solver ode113 capable of high accuracy even
for smooth curves. However, it is not guaranteed that all elements in the derived rate-
based model will occur at the same timescale. This could apply, if the vapor flow rate is
fast compared to the reaction rate, or vice versa. When the system becomes inherently
stiff, the non-stiff solver starts to become slow, ineﬀicient and error prone. This is espe-
38
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
cially a problem when simulating the MTBE and DME case, where ode113 would produce
numerical errors that could crash the simulation, unless the relative error tolerance in the
odeoptions has been set to a very small value. Through preliminary probing it is found
that the stiff solver ode15s could eﬀiciently and robustly solve model equations. The main
issue with using ode15s to solve the ODE system, is that the speed of the solver greatly
decrease as the number of equations increase. This corresponds to adding more trays
in the simulated column. It is possible to partially mitigate this effect by introducing
a sparse Jacobian matrix, which alleviated the computational effort in computing the
gradients.
3.4
Model Verification
This thesis project aims at investigating dynamic parameter effects on a complex system
by solely evaluating simulated behavior. It is therefore paramount that the mathematical
interpretation and technical implementation are credible before any useful conclusion can
be drawn from generated simulation results [53].
There are generally two different methods which can be employed to establish trust
towards any simulation model, namely validation or verification [53, 54]. A graphical
representation of the various terms, and there mutual connection, in coupling real-world
dynamics to simulated interpretations are depicted by Pace [54] in Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Real-world and simulation-world relationships in developing system theories and
simulation models with verification and validation. Experiment objectives should be derived
from validated requirements. Validation is always relative to objectives/requirements/intended
use. The dotted red lines imply comparison, assessment, or evaluation. (Diagram developed
and copyrighted by Dr. R. G. Sargent, Syracuse University, Jan 2001) [54]
Validation refers to either a direct comparisons between experimental- and simulated
39
==PAGE:52==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
results, or a conceptional evaluation of the derived mathematical model, and its fidelity
towards system theories established from previous physical experimentation [54]. Thus,
if a simulation model is able to either mirror the experimental results, or comply with
system theories, credibility towards the simulated results should be established.
Verification instead focus on establishing trust towards the model implementation.
This evaluation can be performed without direct access to experimental data. Instead,
it requires that simulation results are accessible in literature, which has previously been
either validated or verified. If the same system theories have been used to derive a concep-
tual model, it should be possible to recreate the published simulation results. Achieving,
such an evaluation should establish a similar trust towards the model implementation as
for the published version [53, 54].
Since, accessible experimental data from literature is scarce and no physical experi-
mentation is conducted in this thesis project, trust towards the simulation model has to
be achieved through verification strategies. Therefore credibility towards this model iter-
ation is established, by replicating previously reported case simulations using the same
system interpretations.
As described in section 3.3, it is possible to segregate the simulation model into
independent elements, consisting of e.g. the thermodynamic property computations- or
column dynamic models. This allows for a more isolated verification study of the indi-
vidual elements, if the simulated results deviate significantly from the published results.
The implementation has been generalized for arbitrary chemical systems, which allows for
easier verification against different case studies. Since the simulations are computed nu-
merically, and the coeﬀicients in the thermodynamic property models have been updated,
the produced results are expected to vary slightly from the published results. However,
it is expected that the simulation results display the same overall trends. Additionally,
the exact design of the separation unit is not explicitly stated for every case. Missing
parameters have been approximated to approach the reported solution from literature.
3.4.1
Case Studies
To generalize the parameter effects of the mathematical models, it is beneficial to conduct
a series of tests for different real-world chemical systems, and evaluate the quality of the
operation as the investigated parameters are changed. Conducting the same test for a
set of chemical systems produce a set of model responses. Compiling these results across
the various chemical systems, it is possible to determine general trends, with greater
confidence. These general trends can then be applied to possibly predict the behavior of
an arbitrary chemical system. The systems implemented as case studies in this project,
are deliberately selected to be kinetically slow. This is because the residence time effects
on the column operation diminish when the chemical reaction becomes fast [38].
The selected case systems have previously been investigated, by Pătruţ [1] and
Nielsen [32].
The published simulation results have in this case been obtained using
model interpretations that mirror the complexity of the mathematical model employed
in this thesis project. Not only will these case studies assist in establishing the credibility
40
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
towards this model implementation, but also allow for more generalized conclusions to
be drawn from the simulation study conducted in Chapter 4.
The three cases investigated, encompass methyl acetat- (MeOAc), dimethyl ether-
(DME) and methyl tert-butyl ether (MTBE) synthesis. The cases are employed as part of
the verification study, but are also used in further simulation analysis. The three product
species are all classified as feed-stock chemicals, meaning they are predominantly used
as either an additive in finished goods or as a reactant in other chemical facilities to
manufacture more complex molecules [55, 56, 57]. Feed-stock chemicals are often priced
relatively cheaply resulting in a narrow profit margin, relying on a large production ca-
pacity in order for the manufacturing companies to profit. Large production capacities
translate to a large energy consumption. The motivation for improving the overall en-
ergy eﬀiciency for the three case studies, therefore have a strong real world applicability.
Additionally, the three case systems are all relatively slow chemical reactions, containing
both chemical equilibria and several non-reactive azeotropes.
The detailed set-up and final model configuration has been printed in Table 3.1 and
Table 3.2 respectively. One will notice a strong deviation from the published simulation
results for the DME and MTBE case in particular. These observations are addressed in
the respective sub-sections below.

3.4
Methyl Acetate
The most common pathway for synthesizing methyl acetate, is to conduct an acid cat-
alyzed esterfication of acetic acid and methanol, producing methyl acetate, and water as
a secondary by-product. The synthesis is shown in eq. (3.2) as a reversible equilibrium
reaction, with relatively slow kinetics.
CH3OH + CH3COOH
H+
−−⇀
↽−−CH3COOCH3 + H2O
(3.2)
In the original patent application, filed by the Eastman Kodak Company, it is en-
visioned to employ a conventionally operated process which utilized sulphuric acid as a
catalyst for synthesizing methyl acetate. sulphuric acid would be fed close to the top of
the column, together with a feed comprised of high purity acetic acid. The second feed,
containing high purity methanol, would be fed close to the bottom of column [19]. As
the less volatile acetic acid trickle down through the column, the more volatile methanol
moves counter-currently up through the column, increasing the overall reactant concen-
tration of both species on the reactive trays. Since produced water and methyl acetate
exhibit a larger relative volatility between them, they separate easily as they are produced
in the reactive section. Thus, a two feed configuration, at either end of the reactive sec-
tion, has been the standard design since the inception of the Eastman process in 1988
[40].
A newer, heterogeneous, alternative to the homogeneous sulphuric acid catalyst has
been presented by Zuo et. al. in 2014 [43]. Having investigated two different acid cation-
exchange resins, they reported that an effective heterogeneous mesh, named NKC-9, could
be installed in conventional reactive columns to substitute the added sulphuric acid all
together. This reactive model is employed in the periodic base case by Nielsen [32] and
is therefore also employed in this thesis, to reduce deviations between the model imple-
mentations. The reaction kinetics for the NKC-9 catalyst has been printed in eq. (3.3).
The heat of reaction is also reported as mildly exothermic at ∆rH = −3016.5 [kJ/kmol].
The investigation conducted by Zuo et. al. concluded that the most eﬀicient use of the
heterogeneous catalyst is achieved by operating the column with a molar feed ratio of
1.2:1 acetic acid:methanol [43]. To ensure a comparable operation for the verification
study it is decided to implement an equimolar feed of the two reactants instead, as previ-
ously performed by Nielsen in 2018 [32]. The two reactants are however, still fed as two
separate streams at either end of the reactive section.
kf = 5.1093 · 1010 · exp
(
−52275.94
RT
)
(3.3a)
Keq = exp
(
2565.1
T
−4.7335
)
(3.3b)
rMeOAc =
kf · [aMeOHaAcOH −aMeOAcaH2O/Keq]
(1 + 4.95aMeOH + 3.18aAcOH + 4.16aMeOAc + 5.24aH2O)2
(3.3c)
43
==PAGE:56==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
The main motivation behind implementing the reactive distillation process for methyl
acetate synthesis is to mitigate the three binary low-boiling point azeotropes identified
within the chemical system [16, 19]. The first pair exist for acetic acid and water at
85.48 mol% and 14.52 mol% respectively. Since the column is designed to operate at
high conversion rates this azeotrope is expected to be easily mitigated in the reactive
section and not limit the water purity of the flow exiting the bottom of the column. The
second azeotrope exist between the methanol and methyl acetate pair at 33.18 mol% and
66.82 mol% respectively. Again, it is expected that a high conversion in the reactive
section will mitigate this azeotrope and not compromise the purity of the methyl acetate
distillate. The final azeotrope occur between water and methyl acetate at 4.05 mol% and
95.95 mol% respectively. Due to the high relative volatility between water and methyl
acetate the species are expected to separate suﬀiciently as the are produced to mitigate
this azeotrope.
44
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
Figure 3.4: Replication of methyl acetate simulation conducted by Nielsen [32]. (top) the
liquid tray composition, (middle) the temperature and vapor velocity profiles and (bottom) the
rate of reaction. All plotted values are taken at the end of the vapor flow period
The column configuration reported by Nielsen [32] has been printed in Table 3.2. It
is possible to recreate the simulation originally presented by Nielsen [32] for the methyl
acetate case. The replication has been illustrated in Figure 3.4. It should be mentioned
that the tray notation in this project is flipped compared to the tray notation used in
the results presented by Nielsen. This is a conscious decision made in this project to
remain consistent with tray notation presented earlier. Therefore, tray one is denoted
as the condenser and tray NT is denoted as the reboiler depicted on e.g. Figure 1.1
or Figure 3.1. A quick assessment of the simulation result printed above confirm that
45
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
the overall system behavior seems to largely agree with the published simulation result.
However, a more thorough evaluation of the results presented in Figure 3.4 and Table 3.2
show a notable deviation on several aspects, including the bottoms composition, reactant
conversion, boil-up ratio and liquid reflux amount.
The conversion of both reactants appears to have dropped by approximately 10%
and the bottoms composition only include 75.7% water compared to the 95.9% reported
by Nielsen [32]. While the distillate purity appears to be in close agreement with Nielsen’s
results, the refluxed amount has decreased from 0.535 to 0.468 [kmol/cycle]. Additionally,
it appears that the most reactive trays have shifted downwards to trays (#18 - #21)
whereas Nielsen previously reported trays (#17 - #19) as the most reactive section in
the column [32].
Since this is the very first verification case study, the simulation includes both an
unverified implementation of the system dynamics, thermodynamic characteristics and
updated parameter values for the thermodynamic property calculations. The source of
the deviation can therefore originate from several different sources. Observing that the
overall shape and magnitude of the composition-, vapor flow rate, temperature and rate
profiles depicted in Figure 3.4 closely resemble published results, it is reasoned that the
dynamic- and thermodynamic model implementations should have been implemented
correctly. The errors still seem rather significant if the deviation is only attributed to a
different set of thermodynamic property coeﬀicients. Reflecting on the group of parame-
ters deviating notably from the published results, it is noticed that all affected parameters
could be somewhat explained by underestimating the vapor flow rate. This would reduce
the boil-up ratio, bottoms purity and result in less material entering the condenser, which
in turn, would decrease the refluxed flow magnitude.
Assessing, the model implementation procedure by Nielsen [32], it is noticed that the
previously mention index error found in Pătruţ’s article, has been included in his model
iteration. The index error is therefore also implemented in this model iteration as well,
and simulation results are recalculated and compared against Nielsen’s published results
again. To avoid confusion with the benchmark case presented in Table 3.2, which is used
later in the analysis, the result containing index errors have been printed in Chapter C.
The recomputed simulation, containing the index error on the vapor flow rate, is
printed in Figure C.1 with operational details listed in Table C.1. Once the index error
is included in the simulation model, the reactant conversion is increased to 93.5% and
the bottoms water purity rise to 88.2%. This still fall a bit short of the published results,
but has definitely improved the agreement with the operation presented by Nielsen [32].
Additionally, it is observed that the most reactive trays have shifted back towards tray
(#18 - #19). This particular case study should reinforce that the thermodynamic model
and reactive model for methyl acetate production has been implemented correctly. The
observation of improved model agreement with published results when an index error is
included, suggest that this model iteration is in agreement with the system interpretation
described in Section 2.4. With a significant deviation from the reference case originating
from the presence of an index error, it suggests that the dynamic model for periodic
operation has been implementation correctly as well.
46
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
The column configuration presented in Table 3.2 are described by Nielsen [32] as
the minimum design configuration. Assuming, that this implies the best setup found in
the published work, any improvements found to his design found in this project would
ultimately continue the developing of methyl acetate synthesis via reactive periodic sep-
aration. Therefore this configuration is used as a benchmark when analyzing the methyl
acetate system later.
DME
Dimethyl ether is an important feed-stock chemical, which has a wide range of applica-
tions. It is most commonly used as either a precursor to producing more complex organic
compounds, or as an additive found in several types of combustible propellant [1]. The
DME synthesis has previously been used as a case study to investigate reactive distilla-
tion [40, 17] and is also the very first chemical system to be assessed for reactive periodic
separation, presented by Pătruţ [1]. Often, the DME synthesis pathway implemented in
industry, conduct a dehydration of methanol as depicted in eq. (3.4).
The rate of the equilibrium limited DME synthesis has been investigated by Song et.
al. in 1998 using a strongly acidic macro porous resin which had a distribution of acidic
reactions sites equal to 4.9 [mmol/g] [60].

formulation, where two molecules of methanol combine to produce DME and water as a

Pătruţ mentions that the ternary system can exhibit phase splitting, if the mole

in the article [1]. A set of updated values have been used and are printed in Chapter A.

Figure 3.5: Replication of DME simulation conducted by Pătruţ [1]. (top) the liquid tray
composition, (middle) the temperature and vapor velocity profiles and (bottom) the rate of
reaction. All plotted values are taken at the end of the vapor flow period
The replication of the DME case study presented by Pătruţ [1] has been illustrated in
Figure 3.5. Apparent from the operating condition, printed in Table 3.2. It is not possible
to recreate the simulation results presented by Pătruţ [1] when the published operating
reflux ratio of 48.31 is employed. In this case, the simulation produce a shift to the
operational condition, described by Pătruţ [1],´ as an unstable design. This design would
produce high purity DME distillate, and a mixture of methanol and water in the bottoms.
Pătruţ [1] classified this operation as unstable, because the operation would diverge upon
small perturbations in the vapor flow rate from the reboiler or reflux ratio. Reducing
49
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
the reflux ratio to 33.31 instead, achieved an operation similar to the published result.
While similar, there still appear significant deviations between the two simulation results,
which is mostly attributed to the lowered reflux ratio. The mole fraction of DME in the
distillate for the replication with a lowered reflux ratio has been reported at 77.9% which
turned out slightly lower than the published 81.7%. The refluxed amount is naturally
lowered from 3.99 to 2.82 [kmol/cycle], due to the decrease in the reflux ratio, meaning
less of the condensed vapor is returned during the LFP. The conversion of methanol has
also been slightly affected, decreasing by approximately 2% for the simulation presented
in Figure 3.5.
Again, it appears that the implemented simulation model is able to accurately recre-
ate the dynamic behavior of the reactive separation, when comparing the depiction on
Figure 3.5 against the published composition, vapor flow rate and temperature profiles.
Since, the shape and magnitude of these illustrations are in close agreement with the
published figures, it is again not likely that the deviations originate from an incorrect im-
plementation of the reaction- or thermodynamic model. Apart from the application of the
Wilson activity model, the other thermodynamic functions are also used in the previous
case study for methyl acetate. Here, it is argued that the thermodynamic model is most
likely implemented correctly and only updated thermodynamic parameters should cause
the deviation between the model employed by Pătruţ [1] and the model implemented
in this project. It is speculated that the implementation of the Wilson activity model
might contain errors, or that the reactive system has been implemented erroneously. It
is however noticed that Nielsen [32] has previously replicated the DME case presented
by Patruct [1], and achieved a more accurate replication. It is in the previous case study
confirmed that the index error is most likely present in the Nielsen [32] model implemen-
tation. Since the index error has also been printed in the published paper, it is suggested
it might also be present in the model implemented, used to produce the simulations
presented by Pătruţ [1].
The index error is, once again, introduced into this implementation of the simulation
model to investigate, if it is present in the model used to produce the published results
by Pătruţ [1].
From the resulting figure printed in Figure C.2 and associated table
Table C.2 it becomes apparent that the deviations is primarily caused by the presence
of an index error, also included in the model implemented by Pătruţ [1]. Increasing the
reflux ratio to 48.31 the methanol conversion is increased to 92.36% resulting in a final
distillate DME molar composition of 85.8%. The refluxed amount is also increased to 3.91
[kmol/cycle]. While deviation on several operational parameters are still present, they
are much smaller and could justifiably occur due to numerical errors, or the application
of different thermodynamic parameter values.
The presence of the different indexing used in the computation of the vapor flow rate
has been shown for the methyl acetate and DME case study to have a significant effect on
the operation of the reactive separation. It is therefore non-trivial that this deviation has
been observed for two model implementations already published in literature. Currently,
this model implementation is in disagreement regarding the interpretation of derived
expression for the vapor flow rate. This makes it diﬀicult to assess, whether the expression
for the vapor flow rate derived in this work, or the expression published by Nielsen
50
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
[32] and Pătruţ [1] are correct. Lack of model validation against experimental results
only increase the diﬀiculty of performing this evaluation. By revisiting the more resent
model derivations for periodic separation by Andersen [33] and Rasmussen [29], a more
detailed expression for the vapor flow rate can be consulted. Assessing, Andersen’s and
Rasmussen’s derivation procedure provide confidence towards the derivation performed
in this project. Here the proposed derivation of the vapor flow rate presented in eq. (2.26)
are in agreement with the indexing used by both Andersen and Rasmussen.
If it is assumed that Pătruţ [1] intended to arrive at the expression for the vapor
flow rate printed in the published article, there would appear inconsistencies in the energy
balance. An example can be illustrated by evaluating a given tray n. Here the energy
of the vapor leaving the tray is computed as the expression, Vn
NC
∑
i=1
∆vapHnyi,n, using the
simplified vapor enthalpy. Due to the assumption of no energy loss in the process, this
energy should arrive at the tray immediately above n. Evaluating the expression for
the vapor energy entering tray n −1 from the tray below will illustrate the error in the
notation used by Pătruţ [1]. Now there exist two different expression for computing the
vapor flow leaving tray n which must equal Vn
NC
∑
i=1
∆vapHnyi,n = Vn
NC
∑
i=1
∆vapHn−1yi,n. This
can only be true if ∆vapHn−1 = ∆vapHn = constant. Since Pătruţ [1] explicitly state
that the heat of vaporization is calculated as a function of the temperature, and the
temperature changes in both time and between trays, the heat of vaporization is indeed
not constant. It is therefore safe to assume that the published expression for the vapor
flow rate does not support the system interpretation in regards to the energy balance.
Therefore, it is decided to treat the indexing used by Nielsen [32] and Pătruţ [1]
as a typographical error which has persisted in the implementation process.
At this
point, the implemented periodic model has consistently agreed with published simulation
results, if the known deviation in indexing for the vapor flow rate is accounted for. Going
forward, it is assumed that the dynamic model for the operation- and the thermodynamic
implementation correctly reflect simulation models and system interpretations described
in detail in Section 2.4.
With confidence established towards the periodic- and thermodynamic model, the
continued analysis of the DME case system will evaluate the reactive system reported
by Song et. al. for methanol dehydration on acidic resin. The benchmark configuration
used in the simulation study remains the same which has been listed in Table 3.2.
MTBE
The MTBE system is initially introduced by Nielsen as a new case study to evaluate
performance of existing and new design algorithms [32]. MTBE is an alternative to lead
based additives used to improve the octane number of gasoline [59]. The common synthe-
sis pathway for producing MTBE, consists of conducting an acid-catalyzed esterfication
of methanol and isobutene as depicted in section 3.4.1.
51
==PAGE:64==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.4
CH3OH + (CH3)2C−−CH2
H+
−−⇀
↽−−(CH3)3COCH3
Al-Jarallah [59] has investigated the kinetics of synthesizing MTBE via. the path-
ways printed in section 3.4.1. He published results for kinetics that are either catalyzed
by a heterogeneous cat-ion exchange resin or homogeneous sulphuric acid [59]. Nielsen
employed the kinetics employing the sulphuric acid catalyst. However, he aimed at em-
ulating the presence of a heterogeneous unit, and Nielsen therefore assumed a specified
concentration of sulphuric acid is present only on selected trays.
This assumption is
addressed by Nielsen whom concluded that while the specific operation might not be
realizable in practice it should be possible to emulate a theoretical system for which his
design algorithm could be tested [32]. The chemical reaction is very exothermic, with the
heat of reaction amounting to ∆rH = −37200 [kJ/kmol]. Therefore, the primary benefit
of implementing this particular reaction in reactive separation equipment, is to utilize
the heat of reaction to aid in the separation of the product species.
kf = k0

(3.8d)
In reality a homogeneous catalytic distillation could be simulated in a number of
different ways. Since sulphuric acid would be the least volatile species in the mixture, the
easiest emulation of a homogeneous reaction could be to assume a constant concentration
of acid, only present on all trays below the catalytic feed tray. Another approach would
be to include the catalyst in the species dynamics as a fourth component. For the purpose
of addressing the main objectives in this thesis project, and to reduce deviations in the
verification procedure, the assumption of heterogeneous behavior is replicated.
The design proved to have increased performance if the feed is located at the bottom
of the reactive section on tray #10. The molar composition of the feed is mirrored from
the simulation test conducted by Nielsen to reduce deviation from previously implemented
simulations [32]. The selection of a tray below the reactive section has been determined
to produce the best operation, since both reactants are substantially more volatile than
the MTBE product.

Figure 3.6: Replication of MTBE simulation conducted by Nielsen [32]. (top) the liquid tray
composition, (middle) the temperature and vapor velocity profiles and (bottom) the rate of
reaction. All plotted values are taken at the end of the vapor flow period
It is noticed that the presented result deviate significantly from the presented case
study by Nielsen [32]. The original design only required two reactive stages and a vapor
flow duration of 10 seconds to achieve almost 99% conversion of the methanol. Scouring
the source code for Nielsen’s [32] implementation of the rate expressions, it is identified,
that the rate expression is reported in incorrect units. Instead of reporting the rate in
units of [kmol/s], he reports the rate in [mol/s], thus greatly exaggerating the rate of
reaction. To validate the implementation of the rate expression in this thesis project, the
original experiments conducted by Al-Jarallah are recreated [59]. Here, it is additionally
53

noticed that the reported pre-exponential factor and activation energy for the forward rate
constant expression, is reported inconsistently when reevaluating the published Arrhenius
plot reprinted from Al-Jarallah’s publication [59].
In Figure 3.7 are the Arrhenius plots for the forwards and backwards reactions pre-
sented together with the experimental results from simulating a reactive batch system
using published- and adjusted values in the kinetic expressions. The specific batch reac-
tion case takes place with an initial molar concentration of 2:1 methanol/isobutene. A
concentration of 1 w% sulphuric acid is added to the mixture and the reaction is carried
out at a constant temperature of 60 [C] for a period of up to 200 [min] [59].
Figure 3.7: Arrhenius plot published by Al-Jarallah to determine (A) forward & (B) backwards
rate constants for sulphuric acid catalyzed methyl tert butyl methanol production.
As depicted in Figure 3.7, the published kinetic values do not align perfectly with
the dynamics of the experimental example. Instead an adjusted value for these kinetic
values appear to more accurately represent the kinetic behavior in the initial 100 [min].
After the 100 [min] mark, the simulated behavior starts to slightly deviate from the final
equilibrium composition. It should be noted that the deviation in the final equilibrium

parameters are determined from the coeﬀicients in the fitted linear expression. In Fig-

until the 100 [min] mark, there is observed an offset in the final equilibrium composition
when employing the adjusted Arrhenius values seen in Table 3.3. From the limited number
of data points available to fit the Arrhenius equation printed in eq. (3.9) the final outcome
is very susceptible to inaccuracy in reading the data points. Throughout this thesis report
the adjusted values are employed rather than the published as higher accuracy in the
dynamic area is preferred when determining the affect of various operational parameter
changes.
55
==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 3. MODEL IMPLEMENTATION
3.5
3.5
Concluding Remarks
The Backwards-Integration design algorithm and fixed-point iteration method have been
introduced as numerical methods which allows for the design and solving of periodic and
conventional reactive distillation units. The technical implementation of mathematical
models in MATLAB© R2020b syntax have been discussed together with the numerically
solvers used to compute the various ODE systems for three implemented case studies.
The final source code used to generate all results presented in this thesis report can be
found in Appendix Chapter B.
The implementation of thermodynamic property models and periodic reactive simu-
lation model have been verified against three, previously conducted, case studies found in
literature. Observed deviations between the simulation results presented in this work and
publications is determined to originate from persistent index errors in the imperviously
implemented models used to generate the simulation results presented in literature.
It is ultimately determined that the model simulation implemented in this thesis
project is coherent with the system interpretation used to derive the mathematical model.
The implementation is determined to have a strong foundation, and it can credibly re-
flect the conceptual models, which are derived from system interpretations discussed in
Section 2.4. It is therefore expected that the results from succeeding simulation exper-
imentation conducted in Chapter 4 can be used to expand the understanding of the
complex system behavior within the limitations dictated by the employed assumptions.
56
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 4
Simulations of the Periodic Reactive
Model
A functional simulation model for the periodic reactive separation has been derived in
Section 2.4 and later verified in Section 3.4. Having established credibility towards the
implementation, by replicating simulation results published in literature, the next step is
to utilize the tool to address the thesis objectives. A thorough investigation of the vapor
flow duration effects, on the quality of column operation, is conducted. Additionally, are
the effects associated with the reflux ratio and vapor flow rate from the reboiler also
investigated.
In this section, emphasis is put on conducting a series of simulations of varying
complexity for different parameter perturbations, and analyze the resulting change in
the pseudo steady state operation. The analysis will pay special attention towards the
operation of the reactive sections of the column, since the main hypothesis states that the
vapor flow period can be utilized, to better control the reactive aspect of the operation.
Additionally, are the parameter effects of liquid hold-up on trays, vapor flow rate from
the reboiler and the reflux ratio, also evaluated in this chapter. The replicated simula-
tions presented in Section 3.4 serve as a benchmark configuration for which the various
parameters are perpetuated.
The first set of tests comprises of single parameter, single tray perturbation simu-
lations. Each tray inside the column are simulated, assuming that there are no coupled
dynamics between the trays. The vapor flow duration, inlet vapor flow rate and initial
liquid holdup are then changed ±10% and the impacts on the operation are evaluated.
Next, the simulations are repeated, including the complex and coupled dynamics of the
reactive column. The final set of simulations are conducted for perturbations in the va-
por flow period duration for different reaction rates. The rates of reaction for the three
case studies are manipulated by changing the amount of catalyst present on each of the
reactive trays.
57
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.1
4.1
Performance Parameters
Performance parameter are introduced in this chapter to quantify, whether a change in
an operational parameter has an overall positive-, negative- or negligible effect on the
column operation.
Reactive columns are in in this project said to operate favorably,
when it achieves a high degree of feed conversion, and a high product purity in either the
distillate or bottom flows. The purity of the distillate and bottoms are readily computed
from the molar hold-up at the end of the VFP. The introduced parameters in this section
therefore aim at providing more insight into the characteristics of the reactive section
instead.
The first performance parameter, is the total amount of reaction that occurred in
the duration of a complete periodic cycle. Since reactions are assumed to only take place
during the vapor flow period this parameter is obtained by integrating the reaction rate
over the vapor flow duration as depicted in eq. (4.1). This parameter has the unit of
[kmol/cycle]. It accounts for variations in the reaction rate over the entire vapor flow
period, which is not included in Pătruţ’s method [1] for determining the amount reacted
on a tray in a cycle. The formulation presented is able to account for both a forwards and
backwards equilibrium reaction. If the parameter is negative, this translate to a mostly
the reverse reaction occurring on that particular tray.
Rj =
∫tV F P
0
rj(t) dt
Ri,j = νiRj
(4.1)
This parameter directly states the extent of reaction achieved on the reactive trays
in the column in a period cycle.
It is then possible to compare all reactive trays to
determine, which tray contributes most to the conversion of the feed. Having specified
the feed input, it is also possible to evaluate, how close the operation of all reactive trays
get to a desired degree of reactant conversion. By evaluating how operational parameters
affect the effectiveness of the reactive section, it might be possible to draw generalized
conclusions regarding the various parameter effects on the possible degree of conversion.
One of the first steps in a process design workflow is to specify the desired degree of
conversion, distillate purity and product recovery. It is then possible to perform a shell
mass-balances to determine the total amount of reaction that must occur in a cycle in
order to achieve a desired degree of conversion. If this can be generalized to a relationship
between the vapor flow period and number of reactive trays for generalized systems, it
has a huge potential for improving the preliminary design of reactive periodic separations,
and their applicability for arbitrary systems.
The ODE-model is solved numerically and therefore the integration has to be per-
formed numerically as well. This is achieved in MATLAB© R2020b by introducing the
trapz function.
The second performance parameter assesses the distance to the chemical equilibrium
at the end of the VFP. It is debated, whether the operation of a reactive distillation unit

4.1
is more eﬀicient if the chemical reaction is allowed to settle at the chemical equilibrium
before the VFP ends. In this case a tray which reach chemical equilibrium could instead
be evaluated using a more simple elemental -based design algorithm [1]. The parameter
is computed by using the chemical equilibrium definition printed in Section 2.2.2 and the
chemical equilibrium from kinetic sources together with the reaction stoichiometry.

the resulting liquid molar holdup should result in a composition where the reaction spe-
cific equilibrium constant, and the reaction quotient, are both identical. This can be
solved as a single non-linear equations, since the change in liquid molar holdup must
obey the stoichiometric coeﬀicient for the particular reaction.

to reaching the equilibrium the value diminish.
This parameter could be diﬀicult to
generalize for arbitrary chemical systems, but it could potentially be used together with
the vapor liquid equilibrium to map various compositions by regions, where the system
is kinetically limited. This would definitely improve the understanding of the general
direction the overall operation, in order to reach an overall more effective operation of
the column.
The third and final performance parameter employed in this chapter, is the mean
Damköhler number. Since the Damköhler number has been used in the design of con-
ventional reactive distillation units, it is theorized that it could potentially be used in
the design of periodic reactive distillation columns as well. Since the Damköhler num-
ber describes a relationship between the reaction kinetics and the residence time, this
would potentially allow for easier generalization for the selection of reflux ration, feed
amount or vapor flow rate from the reboiler. This depends on the specific formulation
and computation of the dimensionless parameter.

Since, the periodic operation of the chemical system more closely resemble the dy-

that the parameter will change over the duration of the VFP. Therefore a mean value
is employed, as depicted in eq. (4.4). In practice this is computed using the MATLAB©
function mean.

presentation by Chen et. al. [39], where the multiplication of the holdup is necessary, since
the extent of reactions is determined in units of [moles reacted/time/mole of mixture].

In the simulation context, the Damköhler number is calculated for each discrete
time point in the numerical integration of the vapor flow period. By evaluating a mean
Damköhler number computed for each tray, this can maybe provide some generalized
insight into the column operation for an overall Damköhler number. It is possible to
compare this value against not only the approach to the chemical equilibrium, but also
the total amount reacted in a column.
4.2
Single Tray - Single Parameter Analysis
The initial simulation study serves as a preliminary investigation into the parameter
effects on individual reactive trays, operated periodically. Using the benchmark configu-
ration printed in Table 3.2, it is possible to compare the individual tray behavior against
different tray locations in the column. While this approach neglects the coupled dynamics
of trays in series, it allows for an easy and quick assessment of the effects of the vapor
flow rate from the reboiler, and liquid hold-up on the tray behavior. To quantify the
comparison between different operational configurations the performance parameters are
implemented for all three case studies.
The benchmark column configurations presented in Table 3.2 have been simulated,
and the pseudo steady state have been obtained. The column operation at the start of the
vapor flow period is then used as the operating benchmark for each tray. Next, the inlet
vapor velocity, Vj+1, initial liquid molar holdup, M (L)
i
, and vapor flow duration, tV FP, are
then, in sequence, changed by ±10%. The change in the performance parameters for the
selected reactive trays are then computed and printed for each of the three case studies.
In the subsections below, are the simulation results for each chemical systems pre-
sented. Afterwards the general observations are discussed briefly.

4.2.1
Methyl Acetate
The previous benchmark simulation depicted on Figure 3.4, show that the rate of reaction
at the end of the VFP is significantly larger at trays close to the bottom of the reactive
section. The trays #10, #16 and #19 have been selected for closer inspection due to the
low, medium and high rate of reaction depicted on Figure 3.4. The trays are expected
to represent the parameter effects on trays situated towards the top, bottom and middle
part of the reactive section of the column. The simulated responses of the individual
trays are depicted in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Single tray pertubation simulations for the methyl acetate case study. (top) Liquid
holdup composition at the end of the VFP. (2) Total amount reacted in VFP. (3) Degree of equi-
librium composition reached at the end of VFP. (4) The mean Damköhler number throughout
the VFP.
61
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.2
The simulation results depicted in Figure 4.1, show that the relative effect of changing
the vapor flow duration has the largest impact on the total amount reacted and the
Damköhler number. Neither of the manipulated variables appear to have had a significant
impact on the approach to the chemical equilibrium.
Tray #19 appears to exhibit a larger susceptibility to changes in the vapor flow
duration, vapor flow rate and initial molar holdup compared to tray #10 and #16. This
tray does also, account for the majority of the total amount reacted in the column,
displaying almost three times total amount reacted compared to tray #16. Tray #10 on
the other hand exhibits virtually no reaction at all. Generally, it appears that increasing
any of the three parameters have increased the total amount reacted on all trays in the
duration of the VFP and vice versa. It is also observed that the total amount reacted
on each tray generally decrease towards the top of the column with tray #10 almost
performing no reaction at all. In this case it would appear that the final reaction rate
depicted on Figure 3.4 accurately denotes the overall reactivity of the trays throughout
the VFP. In this case it could be speculated, if one could achieve a similar degree of
conversion and separation quality, if the reactive stages between the rectifier and tray
#11 are converted to pure separation stages.
Changes in the extent towards the chemical equilibrium at the end of the VFP
appears to change very little for any of the investigated trays. Only a small response can
be observed for manipulations on the vapor flow rate for tray #19. Here, the approach to
the chemical equilibrium is decreased or increased by approximately 1% when the vapor
flow rate is increased or decreased by 10% respectively. Overall the methyl acetate case
study does achieve a chemical equilibrium for tray #10, and decrease towards the bottom
to 94% for tray #16 and 66% for tray #19.
The Damköhler number is found to be only significantly affected by perturbations
in the vapor flow duration. The largest observed values are found for the tray closest
to the top section which agrees with the notion that a large Damköhler number favors
the chemical equilibrium which is indeed reached for tray #10. This also agrees with a
decrease in the dimensionless value looking at tray #16 for which the chemical equilibrium
is not reached. However, the value rises sligtly for tray #19 which exhibit the largest
deviation from the chemical equilibrium. This is not consistent with the expectations
elaborated upon in Section 2.5.1.
4.2.2
DME
Displayed in Figure 3.5, the final rate of reaction for the DME synthesis is almost evenly
distributed throughout the entire reactive section, with a small decrease in the reaction
rate towards the bottom of the column.
The selected trays of interest are therefore
specified to be evenly spaced out throughout the reactive section, selected as tray #5, #7
and #9. It is also noticed that the liquid composition of the holdup primarily consist of
methanol with an increasing water fraction towards the stripper section.

==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL

Single tray pertubation simulations for the DME case.

The simulation results for the selected trays are depicted in Figure 4.2, and clearly
show that the VFP duration has the predominant effect on the total amount reacted, and
the Damköhler number. It is also evident from the depicted simulations, that the chemical
equilibrium is not remotely reached at the end of the VFP for any of the evaluated trays.
Tray #5 displays the largest total amount reacted in the VFP at approximately
11.2 · 10−3 [kmol/cycle] for the benchmark case. This is slightly more than the 10.2 · 10−3
63
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.2
[kmol/cycle] observed for tray #7, and 7.9 · 10−3 [kmol/cycle] observed for tray #9. The
decreasing reactivity down through the reactive section generally agrees with the final
reaction rate observed for the benchmark simulation printed in Figure 3.5. It is also
noticed that the results from a ±10% perturbations in the vapor flow period duration
has a greater impact on the total amount reacted for tray #5 and #7 compared to tray
#9. This is also observed for the previous case, where the trays with a larger amount
reacted in the VFP for the benchmark case are more susceptible to perturbations in the
design variables.
Figure 4.2 shows that no trays are able to reach more than 3.8% approach towards the
chemical equilibrium within the VFP duration for any perturbations. This agrees with
Hossinijad [58] interpretation that the chemical system is virtually a slow irreversible
forwards reaction. Evaluating the molar composition depicted at the top of Figure 4.2,
it is noticed that the mole fraction of DME in the liquid holdup at the end of the VFP
is virtually zero on all trays apart from the two trays closest to the condenser top. The
quick evaporation of the product relative to the rate of the reaction therefore virtually
removes the inhibiting effect of the reverse reaction.
There appears to also be some correlated behavior between the amount reacted and
the distance to the chemical equilibrium at the end og the VFP. The presence of a larger
mole fraction of water on trays closer to the bottom, increase the rate of the reverse
reaction. This would ultimately support operations which do not reach the chemical
equilibrium, but rather benefit greatly from decent removal of the product species.
The Damköhler number appears to increase down the column as the total amount
reacted decrease. It is also noticed the chemical equilibrium is approached as the Damköh-
ler number increases. This agrees with the kinetic effects reported by Venimadhavan [38],
where increasing the Damköhler number would produce a residue curve map that resem-
bled a reactive distillation approaching a equilibrium behavior. This observation strongly
support the idea that the Damköhler number can be employed to predict the performance
of the reactive section inside the column.

The final case study is the MTBE synthesis which has been presented in Table 3.2 and
illustrated for the benchmark simulation in Figure 3.6. It is shown in the benchmark sim-
ulation result that the column appears to have a very reactive top section which decrease
towards the bottom of the column with a small kink around tray #9 and #10. The trays
selected for thorough investigation therefore reflect these various sections, denoting tray
#4, #6 and #9.

that the vapor flow rate has very little influence on the system behavior compared to the
initial molar holdup and the vapor flow duration. This is especially observed for the total
amount reacted and the Damköhler number. This is most likely attributed to the volume
dependency in the MTBE rate expression presented by Al-Jarallah [59]. Therefore an
increase in the initial molar holdup would increase the rate of reaction overall, agreeing
with the observed trend on the figure above. It can also be noticed when comparing the

trays against each other that is suggested there is a high reactivity at bottom and top of

succeeding section.

for any of the parameter perturbation tests.
The decreasing effect of the parameter

at the Damköhler number. In fact, the dimensionless variable seems to follow no overall
trend when comparing against the total amount reacted on the trays. However, it is

value observed for tray #4 and #7. Combined with the results from the approach to the
chemical equilibrium printed above the low Damköhler number can maybe be associated
with the decrease in extent towards the chemical equilibrium.
4.2.4
General Observations
The preliminary study has provided some insight into the various parameter effects on
selected trays for all three case studies. While the case studies differ significantly from
each other, they do exhibit comparable behavior in some instances.
The most notable trend, which occurred for all cases is that increasing the vapor flow
period duration resulted in an increase of the total amount reacted in a cycle and vice
versa. The magnitude of the change in the total amount reacted on the trays appeared
to depend on the amount present for the benchmark case.
It is also observed that only the trays which did not reach chemical equilibrium at the
end of the VFP showed any changes when the vapor flow rate is manipulated. The best
example is depicted on Figure 4.1 tray #19, where the total amount reacted is increased
in comparable magnitudes to the observed effect of the vapor flow duration. Otherwise,
the predominant parameter effects is observed for changes in the vapor flow duration. The
simple tray simulations did also show that simply scaling the liquid molar holdup without
changing the molar composition had a very little effect on the overall tray dynamics apart
from the MTBE case. Changing the initial molar holdup in the MTBE case exhibited a
susceptibility of equal magnitude to perturbation in the vapor flow duration. It is argued
that this behavior originates from the kinetic expressions having a volumetric dependency,
which increased the reactivity with increases in the initial molar holdup.
The results coupled to the Damköhler number appeared to change depending on
the particular case evaluated. In some cases a trend appeared for which a consistent
decrease in the mean Damköhler number correlated with an increase in the total reacted
amount. Also trend could be observed, where a decrease in the mean Damköhler number
correlated with a decrease in the approach towards the chemical equilibrium. Neither of
these trends have been consistent across all three cases. It is investigated in the succeeding
set of simulations, if a more consistent correlations appear for the Damköhler number and
66
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.3
other performance parameter, when the coupled dynamics are included.
4.3
Complete Column - Single Parameter Analysis
Next, a similar set of simulation tests are conducted for the complete reactive distillation
unit. Here, the manipulated variables have to be selected in slightly different way. Since
the behavior of the vapor flow rate and the liquid hold up are now coupled to the dynamics
of other trays, it is no longer possible to manually specify these parameters arbitrarily
for individual trays. If it is desired to investigate the dynamic effects associated with
manipulating the vapor flow rate or initial liquid holdup for only a specific tray, it is
possible to manipulate these indirectly.
By adding a vapor or liquid feed stream at
the particular tray in question, this would indirectly increase the vapor flow rate and
initial liquid holdup. If the composition of the feed, mirrors the composition on the tray,
the addition of the feed would only affect the vapor flow rate or initial liquid holdup.
However, it is much more common to adjust the vapor flow rate throughout the column,
by changing the inlet vapor flow rate from the reboiler instead. The second parameter
manipulated for the complete column simulations is the reflux ratio, which specify the
fraction of condensed vapor returned from the top. This will ultimately increase the
initial liquid holdup on each tray at the start of the VFP.
The reflux ratio, for the periodic operation, has previously been introduced in Sec-
tion 2.4, and comprise a ratio between the evaporated content returned to the column
relative to the amount leaving in the distillate. This parameter is often manipulated in
distillation processes to control the distillate purity of the most volatile components. By
adjusting this parameter in the context of periodic operation, the initial liquid holdup at
the start of the vapor flow period is affected. If the reflux ratio is increased, the amount
of liquid entering the tray below the condenser is increased and vice versa. This will
propagate down through the column, and it is of great interest to investigate how this
parameter affects reactive aspect of the column.
The effects of changing the vapor flow period duration, and changing the vapor flow
rate from the reboiler will affect the boil-up ratio defined in eq. (2.32). If the vapor flow
rate is assumed constant in the duration of the vapor flow period, it is possible to isolate
the specific effect of changing the period cycle. Another issue to address is that the feed
inlet is presented in units of [kmol/cycle]. If this parameter is not scaled accordingly when
the vapor flow duration is changed, the overall column throughput in units of [kmol/h]
will change as well. Since the production capacity of any facility is a very important
design parameter, it is therefore decided to ensure a constant throughput by scaling the
feed as the vapor flow period duration is changed.
It is also important to acknowledge that the selection of the vapor flow duration has
important implications on the operation of the reboiler if the vapor flow rate is assumed
constant. An increase in the vapor flow duration would also increase the molar content
evaporated from the repboiler. If the vapor flow duration is too long, issues could arise
regarding tray or reboiler emptying which would incline the operation to fail. The upper
limit for the period selection can therefore not exceed a value, which given a vapor flow
67
==PAGE:80==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.3
rate from the reboilier exceed total liquid input from the condenser or feeds, presented
expression in eq. (4.6).
tV FP <

VNT
(4.6)
New performance parameters are also introduced for the complete column simulation,
which more accurately reflect the design criteria utilized in regular column design tasks.
This include, a total amount reacted in the reactive section, and the purity of the top and
bottoms. The total amount reacted in the column is computed as the sum of individual
tray contributions. Comparing this against the content leaving the column in the distillate
and bottoms it is possible to determine a degree of conversion for the feed entering the
column. From the pseudo steady state mass balance, the ratio between feed-stock leaving
the column in the bottoms and distillate compared against the inputs, must denote the
total amount converted.

reboiler also changes composition in time, the reference value has been selected as the
composition at the end of the VFP. Since the LFP follows immediately after, this would
be the specific composition at which the distillate and bottoms are drained from the
column.

on vapor flow duration, inlet vapor flow from reboiler and reflux ratio, for three reactive

same effect on the specific parameter. This is observed for the mean Damköhler number,
and the approach to the chemical equilibrium, where both an increase or decrease in the
vapor flow rate from the reboiler, and reflux ratio will results in an overall increase in
both performance parameters. This also contradict the observations from the single tray
analysis. Also deviating from the single tray simulations are the negligible impact of per-
tubating the vapor flow duration regarding both the distance to the chemical equilibrium
and Damköhler number.

Figure 4.5: Simulation results for a ±10% perturbation in tV FP for the complete column
for the methyl acetate case. (1) Total amount reacted on the trays, achieved in one cycle. (2)
Degree of chemical equilibrium reached at the end of the vapor flow period. (3) Mean Damköhler
number throughout the vapor flow period.
Figure 4.5 display the system effects of manipulating the vapor flow duration for all
reactive trays in the column. As indicated on Figure 4.4 the only significant impact the
pertubation has on the investigated performance parameters, pertain the total amount
reacted on each tray. The shape of the reaction profile does not change as the vapor
flow duration is increased or decreased. The total amount reacted on each tray however,
does change. It does however appear that the distribution of the most reactive trays is
unaffected by the perturbations. As previously discussed, will an increase in the vapor
flow duration result in an increase of the amount fed per. cycle in order to maintain a
fixed production capacity. It is therefore speculated that the increase in the total amount
reacted throughout the entire column, increase equivalently to the increased amount fed
per cycle.

==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.3
Figure 4.6: Simulation results for a ±10% perturbation in VNT for the complete column for the
methyl acetate case. (1) Total amount reacted on the trays, achieved in one cycle. (2) Degree of
chemical equilibrium reached at the end of the vapor flow period. (3) Mean Damköhler number
throughout the vapor flow period.
The simulation results for the perturbations in the vapor flow rate from the reboiler
has been depicted in Figure 4.6. It is immediately noticed that the profile of the total
amount reacted on each tray in one cycle shifts towards the top of the column when the
inlet vapor flow rate is increased and vice versa. The effect of manipulating the vapor
flow rate on the reactivity of the various trays have previously been showed, in Section 3.4
when the impact of the index error in the vapor flow rate expression presented by Pătruţ
[1] is discussed.
It is also noticed that the chemical equilibrium is approached, for both a decrease
and increase in the inlet vapor flow. In the case for increased vapor flow rate, this observa-
tion could be explained, by the increase in total amount reacted, which would intuitively
result in the creation of more product, which would reduce the distance to the equilib-
rium composition. It is more diﬀicult to explain why the reduction of the vapor flow rate
and subsequent reduction in the amount reacted would result in a closer aﬀinity to the
chemical equilibrium. This could perhaps be a result of the equilibrium constant being
substantially decreased compared to the benchmark value. Since the tray temperature
72
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.3
is calculated using an assumption of constant saturation temperature the increased con-
centration of volatile species would ultimately reduce the chemical equilibrium constant
used to determine the distance to the chemical equilibrium. It is however, most likely
caused by a decrease in the composition of methanol and water on the top trays which
reduce the rate of both the forward and reverse reaction to approximately zero.
Figure 4.7: Simulation results for a ±10% perturbation in reflux ratio for the complete column
for the methyl acetate case. (1) Total amount reacted on the trays, achieved in one cycle. (2)
Degree of chemical equilibrium reached at the end of the vapor flow period. (3) Mean Damköhler
number throughout the vapor flow period.
A similar plot is constructed for the effects of changing the reflux ratio, depicted in
Figure 4.7. Some of the trends observed for perturbation in the vapor flow rate from the
reboiler are also observed for the perturbations in the reflux ratio. This includes a shift in
tray reactivity, where an increase in the reflux ratio would shift the reactivity towards the
bottom of the column and vice versa. This is consistent with a proportional increase in the
liquid flow which is collected at the column bottom. An increase in chemical equilibrium
is also observed for both an increase and a decrease in the reflux ratio. The magnitude of
this impact, is however substantially smaller compared to similar perturbations for the
vapor flow rate.

printed in Table 4.1 for all perturbations for easier comparison. The purity of methyl

From Table 4.1 it appears that a ±10% change in vapor flow duration increase and
decrease the total amount reacted in the entire column by approximately ±10% as well.
Since, the feed per cycle is scaled by an equal percentage to maintain the production
capacity, the change in feed conversion is less than 0.5%. Evaluating the impact of the
VFP duration changes on the top and bottom purities, it is evident that these are virtually
unchanged as well. This result suggest that operating a methyl acetate column at shorter
VFP durations with smaller liquid holdups are equivalent to operating the column at
longer VFP durations with a larger liquid holdup.
The distillate purity does not change substantially for any of the simulations apart
from the 10% decrease in the vapor flow rate from the reboiler which reduced the methyl
acetate purity of the top by ≈4.5%. This very conservative change may originate from the
distillate already being virtually pure methyl acetate. The lack of susceptibility towards
negative changes, may indicate that the operation is using an excess heat duty on top
purification.
The bottoms purity of water however, illustrate the opposite, where changes in the
reflux ratio and vapor inlet from the reboiler has a significant impact on the water purity
of the bottoms. A 10% increase in the vapor inlet from the reboiler is able to improve
the bottoms purity by almost 21%. This is also reflected, in the increase in total amount
reacted due to the effective return of unreacted methanol and acetic acid to the column.
4.3.2
DME
Pătruţ [1] reported the existence of a threshold reflux liquid flow, L1, for which the pseudo-
steady state operation became unstable. Selecting operations above this threshold would
74
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.3
result in open-loop instability, where small step-changes in manipulated variables would
result in divergence. Since this implementation manipulated L1 indirectly by specifying
the reflux ratio, it is very possible that one of the perturbations can exceed the threshold,
and produce an unstable pseudo-steady. Otherwise, are the trays #5, #7 and #9 evalu-
ated for the complete column simulation, to compare this simulation against the single
tray simulation correspondingly.
The production capacity of the benchmark DME case, presented in Table 3.2, is
specified to handle a 9.0 [kmol/h] methanol feed. The vapor flow rate from the reboiler
is specified at 90.0 [kmol/h]. The printed value for the VFP duration, vapor flow rate
from the reboiler and reflux ratio are perturbated by ±10%. The simulated parameter
values have been listed below.
• tV FP = 60 ± 6 [s]
• VNT = 90.0 ± 9.00 [kmol/h]
• RR = 33.310 ± 3.331 [-]
