4.3.4
General Observations
Comparing the simulation results against those obtained for the single tray simulations, it
is clear that the coupled dynamic behavior is much more complex than initially indicated
by the single tray simulations. Not only does the vapor flow rate and reflux ratio have
a tremendously greater impact on the overall operation than originally indicate by the
single trays. Additionally, the individual trays exhibited varying behavior for changes in
the operational variables, identifying that a particular tray could increase in total amount
reacted from both a positive and negative change in the operational variable.
The detailed figures for the perturbation in vapor flow duration show that the total
amout reacted for the meathyl acetate and DME case scale almost linearly and has little
to no impact on either the distance to equilibrium or the Damköhler number.
The MTBE case is however affected differently, which might be attributed to the
reactive system having a holdup dependency in which the rate of rection increase with
the liquid holdup. Since the feed amount is scaled according to the selection of the vapor
flow duration, this relationship appears to most likely not be linear. The impact on the
rate of reaction from the changes in the liquid holdup is also apparent for the mean
Damköhler number which is significantly impacted displayed in Figure 4.13.
85
==PAGE:98==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.4
The detailed results for perturbations in the vapor flow rate from the reboiler and
the reflux ratio for the methyl acetate and DME case presented in Figures 4.6, 4.7, 4.10
and 4.11. The results printed in these figures show correlated behavior between changes
in the Damköhler number, distance to chemical equilibrium and total amount reacted. It
appears that perturbations which result in increased Damköhler numbers, also result in
closer distance to the equilibrium and reduced total amount reacted on the trays. These
results are in strong agreement with the definition initially observed for the single tray
simulations and reported by Venimadhaven [38].
However, due to observations of contradictory behavior, it should be questioned if
the changes in Damköhler number and other performance parameters are correlated or
appear by coincidence.
4.4
Complete Column - Reaction Rate Effects
The final set of simulations, conducted in this project, aim at investigating the system
behavior when the vapor flow duration is changed for different rates of reaction. This is
achieved for the three case systems by increasing or decreasing the amount of catalyst on
the reactive trays, which is synonymous with increasing or decreasing the rate of reaction
respectively.
It has been observed for the methyl acetate and DME cases that the total amount
of reaction taking place inside the column would scale almost equivalently with the per-
turbations of the vapor flow duration. One theory is that the kinetics of the chemical
reactions are too fast or too slow, causing it to be largely unaffected by the shift in
residence time. To investigate this more closely the rate of the reaction kinetics are artifi-
cially reduced and increased, by changing the amount of catalyst present on the reactive
trays. It is decided to conduct a set of seven simulations for the three cases, where the
benchmark catalyst content is reduced by 10%, 50% and 90%. In case of increasing the
catalyst content, it is increased by 10%, 50% and 1000%. Each setup has been then sim-
ulated for the benchmark operation and a ±10% change in the vapor flow duration. The
system response could then be compiled and investigated if the change in total amount
reacted in the column cycle is indeed scaled for substantially slower and faster reaction
rate kinetics. The case simulation for DME has not been able to account for a 1000%
increase in the amount of catalyst and has therefore been left blank on the designated
spot in Figure 4.17.
Two new performance parameters are introduced to assist in evaluating the simula-
tion results. The first addition is to evaluate the ratio between the total amount reacted
in the column in on cycle, against the benchmark results:
∆Rtot =
∑NT
j=1 Rj
∑NT
j=1 Rbm,j
Benchmark : Rbm,j
(4.9)
If the parameter remains constant for different reaction rates, it will illustrate that
86
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.4
the relative changes in total amount reacted is unaffected by increase of decrease in
the rate of the reaction. The second parameter introduced in this section is the total
conversion of the key feedstock.
This parameter serve mostly as a confirmation that
the perturbation does not affect the relative change in the amount reacted, relative to
the feeding rate printed in eq. (4.7). If deviations are observed, this would translate
to a disproportionate consumption of feedstock as the vapor flow duration is changed,
meaning that operating at long or short period duration would not be equivalent. In
summation, both parameters serve to evaluate if the observed scaling effect persist when
the rate of the reactions are increased and subsequently decreased.
4.4.1
Methyl acetate
The methyl acetate case study exhibited the apparent equivalent scaling for perturbations
in the VFP duration for both the single tray and complete column simulations depicted
in Figures 4.1 and 4.5. The production capacity for the set of simulations are selected
as the benchmark case identical to the case used in the complete column simulations
conducted previously. The various flows are thus fixed at a 7.5 [kmol/h] methanol feed,
a 7.5 [kmol/h] acetic acid feed and a vapor flow rate from the reboiler at 16.5 [kmol/h].
The various amount of catalyst packing used in the simulations presented on Figure 4.16
have been printed in Table 4.4.

Figure 4.16: Complete column simulation for methyl acetate employing various rates of reac-

Figure 4.16 show that the effects on the column behavior, when perturbating the
VFP duration for different rates of reaction.
It appears, that increasing the rate of
reaction has a very little impact on the total amount reacted in the column, and the
final degree of feed conversion. Decreasing the rate of reaction however, does exhibit a
strong impact on the total amount reacted and final degree of feed conversion. While
the effects are small for the 50% catalytic packing it becomes very substantial when the
catalyst packing is decreased to 2.5 [kg]. In this case the total amount reacted in the
88
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL

column, decrease by ≈0.7 [kmol/cycle], and the degree of conversion drops below 60%.
Comparing the changes in the total amount reacted against the benchmark value it is
noticed that all configurations result in a ±10% change, which is equivalent to the ±10%
change in the VFP duration. This support the observations from the previous complete
column simulation that the changes in the vapor flow duration has no effect on the column
operation if the feed is scaled to meet a fixed production capacity.
The column averaged Damköhler number also appears to be largely unaffected for
changes in the VFP duration, for any of the rates of reaction investigated. However,
it is noticed that the benchamark Damköhler number increase as the reaction becomes
faster. This is complicit with the definition presented in Section 2.5.1. The total amount
reacted in the column, or the degree of feed conversion appear to not change significantly,
despite a very large increase in the rate of reaction for a ten fold increase in the amount of
catalyst on the trays. This could indicate that the operation of the reactive column has an
upper limit which is not necessarily situated at the case where the chemical equilibrium
is reached.

Figure 4.17: Complete column simulation for DME employing various rates of reaction. (1)
Total amount reacted in entire column in one periodic cycle. (2) The degree of feed conversion.
(3) A mean Damköhler number for all reactive trays in the column. (4) The relative change in
the total amount reacted as tV FP is perturbated by ±10%.
The result presented in Figure 4.17 largely mirror the observations from the methyl
acetate case. As the rate of reaction is increased the total amount reacted and degree
of feed conversion is virtually unaffected, while a decrease to 12.5 [kg] or 2.5 [kg] yield
significantly different column behavior. In these cases, the total amount reacted for the
benchmark decreased by approximately 2.5·10−2 [kmol/cycle] and 5.5·10−2 [kmol/cycle]
respectively. The degree of feed conversion is also decreased to 55% and 12% respectively.
Comparing the changes in the total amount reacted for all investigated rates of reaction,
90
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.4
it is noticed that a similar ±10% perturbations in the VFP duration results in a ±10%
change in the total amount reacted.
Confirmed by the degree of feed conversion, it
appears that changes in the VFP duration does not affect the operation of the DME
column either. This is identical to the observations made for the methyl acetate case in
the previous section and suggest that the VFP duration can be selected arbitrarily if the
feed is scaled to meet the fixed production capacity.
The Damköhler does increase with an increasing rate of reaction. It is also noticed
that for values ¯
Da > 1.2, the total amount reacted in the column changes insignificantly
despite increases in the Damöhler number. This is also observed for the methyl acetate
case and support the idea that there exist a Damköhler number that denotes the maximum
possible degree of feed conversion achieveable. Venimadhavan [38] showed that it is often
not required to let the Damköhler number become infinitely large, before the operation
settled at the chemical equilibrium RCM. It has been observed for the complete column
simulation in Figure 4.9 that the chemical equilibrium is not remotely achieved during the
perturbations in tV FP. This supports the idea that achieving the chemical equilibrium is
not unanimous with operating obtaining a chemical equilibrium on all reactive trays.
4.4.3
MTBE
Finally the simulation is also conducted for the MTBE case.
Again, the production
capacity for the set used in the complete column simulations are employed as benchmark
for this simulation as well. The various flows are thus specified to handle a 1.35 [kmol/h]
methanol and 3.15 [kmol/h] isobutene feed and a vapor flow rate from the reboiler at
25.6 [kmol/h]. The various amount of catalyst packing used in the simulations presented
on Figure 4.18 have been printed in Table 4.6.

==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.4
Figure 4.18: Complete column simulation for MTBE employing various rates of reaction. (1)
Total amount reacted in entire column in one periodic cycle. (2) The degree of feed conversion.
(3) A mean Damköhler number for all reactive trays in the column. (4) The relative change in
the total amount reacted as tV FP is perturbated by ±10%.
Figure 4.18 presents the simulation results for the MTBE case. Immediately it is
noticed that some of the column behavior strongly deviate from both the methyl acetate
and DME case studies presented in Figures 4.16 and 4.17. The total amount reacted
appears be virtually unaffected, when increasing the rate of the reaction. If the rate is
instead decreased, the total amount reacted and degree of feed conversion also decrease,
which is identical to the behavior observed for both methyl acetate and DME previously.
However, in contrast to the previous case studies, the degree of feed conversion appears to
92
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.5
be affected by perturbations in tV FP. This is especially observed for the 0.9 [mol/L] case
depicted in Figure 4.18. It is also noticed for the benchmark value for the total amount
reacted and degree of feed conversion slightly increase when the amount of catalyst is
increased to 10 [mol/L]. The change is however, very small amounting to less than 1.0·10−3
[kmol/cycle] and 2.0% respectively compared against the catalyst packing of 1.0 [kg].
The most obvious deviation in the behavior between the three case studies is the
uneven relative change in the total amount reacted in the column. As depicted in Fig-
ure 4.18 (4) the ±10% perturbation in the VFP duration does not necessarily produce
a symmetric change in the total amount reacted in the column. This is especially true
for the 1.0 [kg] and 10 [mol/L] catalyst packing cases, where a +10% increase in tV FP
results in a ≈12% and ≈8% increase in the total amount reacted respectively. It is also
noticed that the impact of chaning the VFP duration depend on the rate of the chemi-
cal reaction. In conclusion, it is not trivial if the MTBE case is operated at a short or
long VFP duration with either a small or large liquid holdup. Additionally, the optimal
selection of tV FP appears to depend on the rate of reaction on the reactive trays in the
column.
4.5
Concluding Remarks
In this chapter is the derived and verified mathematical model for the periodic reactive
distillation column employed to conduct three different types of simulation for various
parameter perturbations. All three case studies introduced in Section 3.4 are investigated
for all simulation tests and used to identify both specific- and generalized system behavior.
The preliminary single tray simulations could only provide limited insight into the
operation of the system upon manipulating the vapor flown duration, initial liquid molar
holdup and the vapor flow rate. Comparing the results against the coupled dynamics of
the complete column simulation, it is evident that the relative effect of the vapor flow
rate and the liquid hold-up has been grossly underestimated when the coupled dynamics
are neglected. The relative changes on the operation, associated with perturbating the
vapor flow period duration is however largely equivalent for the two sets of simulations.
It is also found that the total amount reacted in the column appeared to scale with
changes in the vapor flow duration decreasing or increasing by ≈10% upon perturbating
the period time by ±10%. This is however not true for the MTBE case which deviated
from this trend. Investigating this behavior for different rates of reaction found that the
methyl acetate and DME cases could be operated at short or long period durations and
maintain the same degree of feed conversion if the production capacity is kept constant.
The Damköhler number exhibited some promising behavior regarding the increase
towards equilibrium for subsequent increases in the dimensionless parameter, especially
seen for the DME case in Figure 4.8.
However, there remained inconsistencies when
various parameter perturbations are performed for the reboiler flow rate and especially
the reflux ratio. Additionally, the computed Damköler number also displayed sensitivity
towards the specific case system.
93
==PAGE:106==
CHAPTER 4. SIMULATIONS OF THE PERIODIC REACTIVE MODEL
4.5
The Damköhler number is computed assuming that each tray behaves as a single
decoupled batch reactors. This may not be an accurate representation for the Damköhler
number for the overall periodic reactive distillation column, as indicated by the significant
deviation between the single tray and complete column simulations. While the parameter
definitely exhibit some interesting properties it should be investigated if the parameter
can be defined in a different way, which include the coupled dynamics of the periodic
reactive column.
94
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 5
Discussion
A mathematical model for the periodic reactive separation has been derived in Section 2.4,
by introducing the same system interpretation and simplifying assumptions originally em-
ployed by Pătruţ [1]. The simulation model is then successfully implemented in MATLAB
R2020b and subsequently verified in Section 3.4, against simulation results available in
literature. The verified simulation model is then employed to conduct a series of simula-
tion studies, investigating the operational effects for a different selections of vapor flow
period duration, vapor flow rates from the reboiler and the reflux ratios.
The most important results obtained during the simulation study, conducted in
Chapter 4, are presented and discussed in this chapter. The results selected for discussion,
are primarily selected in regards to their applicability in formulating design strategies for
the periodic reactive distillation unit.
Therefore, are the effects of the vapor flow period duration on the column operation
initially presented and discussed in regards to design of a general column. The second
group of results discussed are the applicability of the Damköhler number in future feasi-
bility studies and design strategies. Finally, are the proposed expansions on the project
work suggested.
5.1
Selection of the Vapor Flow Period Duration
The main objective of the simulation studies presented in Chapter 4 have been to expand
the understanding of the design parameter effects on the behavior of the periodic reactive
column. In this regard, special attention has been paid to the effects associated with
changing the vapor flow period duration. The idea is, to use the generalized behavior
across the three case studies to formulate a strategy for selecting the vapor flow duration,
for which no general strategy currently exists.
The simulation studies are successful in consistently reporting increases in the total
amount reacted in the column for increases in the vapor flow period duration, and vice
versa. It is found that when the production capacity of the column is kept constant,
95
==PAGE:108==
CHAPTER 5. DISCUSSION
5.2
the changes in the total amount reacted for the heterogeneous cases are equivalent to
the changes applied in the vapor flow duration. This means that a column operating at
a short cycles with a small feed amount per cycle, is equivalent to a column operating
at long cycles with a large feed amount per cycle. The proposed ability of the periodic
reactive column to achieve a higher degree of feed conversion by increasing the vapor
flow period duration [13] has been disputed for the heterogeneous cases. This behavior
is confirmed to be independent of the rate of the chemical reaction or the magnitude of
the total amount reacted in the benchmark condition.
The behavior is not observed for the homogeneous MTBE case.
It is suggested
that the observed behavior for the heterogeneous cases are caused by the reaction rate
expression including a dependency on the liquid holdup volume. This suggests that a
non-trivial selection of the vapor flow duration cannot be made for the heterogeneous
cases before the liquid holdup dynamics are included into the system interpretation.
Currently the only strategy for selecting the vapor flow duration, is to ensure that
the weeping velocity of the column is met. Given a total amount evaporated from the
reboiler per cycle, the adequate vapor flow period duration is then selected accordingly.
If instead the vapor flow rate leaving the reboiler is specified, the extreme upper limit
of the vapor flow duration is constrained by the expression printed in eq. (4.6). At the
current level of the model complexity it is rather the magnitude of the vapor flow rate vs.
the feed rate which dictate the degree of conversion and general operation of the reactive
section. While the vapor flow duration can be specified to increase or decrease the molar
boil-up in the period the amount it is this ratio which ultimately dictate the quality of
the operation. Unless the reaction rate expression contains a dependency on the liquid
hold-up observed for homogeneous reactions.
5.2
Damköhler Number
The Damköhler number has previously been successfully employed in the design of conven-
tional reactive distillation to determine configurations that result in a favorable column
operation [39, 40]. The prospect of implementing the dimensionless parameter in more
generalized design strategies for the reactive periodic column has been investigated in
parallel with the various parameter effects on the column behavior. The Damköhler num-
ber for the individual trays in the periodic reactive column are modeled equivalently to
batch reactors depicted in eq. (2.33) [38].
The correlation between the mean Damköhler number and the approach towards
the chemical equilibrium or the total amount reacted in a cycle, appears to change sub-
stantially depending on the specific case. Generally, it also appears that the correlation
dependent strongly on the specific trays evaluated. The changes associated with different
design parameters appear to also differentiate the changes in the dimensionless param-
eter disproportionate with either the approach to the chemical equilibrium or the total
amount reacted on the trays in one cycle.
However, evaluating the mean Damköhler number for the entire column showed
96
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 5. DISCUSSION
5.3
a general trend, where the increasing Damköhler number correlated with an increase
in the total amount reacted in the entire column. This correlation is exhibited for all
cases when the rate of reaction is changed. This indicate that there may exist some
average Damköhler number that could potentially be employed to specify a favorable ratio
between the vapor flow rate from the reboiler relative and the catalytic packing. At the
current level of understanding it is not possible to specify an explicit relationship between
the average Damköhler number and the column design. An important aspect observed
for the simulations evaluating various rates of reaction, is the diminishing impact of the
Damköhler number on the total amount reacted once it reaches a threshold value. This
is in agreement with the general kinetic behavior observed in simple batch experiments
[38].
It is also evident from the column simulations that some configurations which ap-
proach the chemical equilibrium result in an overall decrease in the total amount reacted
on the particular trays. This indicate that achieving chemical equilibrium on the trays
are not necessarily synonymous with achieving an optimal operation of the column.
5.3
Proposed Expansions on Project Work
This thesis project concluded that the the vapor flow period duration can be arbitrarily
selected for heterogeneous systems if the vapor flow rate from the reboiler is specified. It
has been determined that the most likely cause is that the mathematical system inter-
pretation does not include a dependency of the liquid holdup. This could be achieved by
including diffusion dynamics for the chemical reaction. Another option could be to in-
clude a hydrodynamic model, where the selection of tray diameter affects the maximum
catalytic packaging possible on the tray. Since the amount of heterogeneous catalyst
directly affect the reaction rate it should be included when determining the column di-
mension if there is a maximum liquid height specification for the trays.
In an attempt to generalize the design across various case studies the Damköhler
number has been computed and assessed in the column simulations for both single tray-
and column perturbations. The implementation and evaluation of the Damköhler number
in feasibility studies for conventionally operated reactive distillation units might suggest
that it could potentially assist in combining a relationship between the amount reacted
and the ability to bypass undesired azeotropes. The prospect of employing this parameter
in design tasks should definitely be investigated further. However, it may aid in the design
process to investigate different approaches in determining the ratio which may fit the
periodic column operation more accurately than simple batch models for the individual
trays. As depicted for the single tray and complete column simulations this approach is
most likely flawed, and could be further improved.
As newer and more complex models are derived there remains a lack of validation
against experimental data. Physical experimentation is a natural extension of the develop-
ment process, to not only determine if the system model is adequate, but also investigate
to which extent the assumptions of equilibrium trays hold. Mass transfer limitations
and more accurate vapor velocity models need to validated. Strong effects observed for
97
==PAGE:110==
CHAPTER 5. DISCUSSION
5.3
changes in the vapor flow rate suggest that feed on higher vapor fraction may allow for
a reduced boil-up ratio. Whether or not this would improve the overall energy eﬀiciency
or general performance of the column should be investigated in more detail.
´
98
==PAGE:111==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
In this report the operational effects of changing the vapor flow period duration for
periodic reactive distillation units have been investigated. The main focus of the thesis
project has been to use the gained insight, to formulate design strategies for the selection
of the vapor flow period duration for an arbitrary chemical system. In this regard the
project is partially successful.
A functional and verified mathematical model for reactive periodic separation has
been successfully implemented and simulated. During the model verification study, it
was discovered that the initial model implementation by Pătruţ et. al. [1] included an
incorrect derivation for the vapor flow rate. This error is shown to have a significant
impact on the simulation results, and is subsequently corrected in this iteration. Three
case studies are conducted, encompassing the synthesis of methyl actetate, dimethyl
ether (DME) and methyl tert-buthyl ether (MTBE). This allow for a comparison of the
operational effects across different chemical systems.
Using the implemented simulation model, a series of perturbations for the vapor flow
period duration are conducted for all three case studies. The simulations show that once
the production capacity and vapor flow rate from the reboiler have been selected, the
vapor flow period duration can be arbitrarily selected for heterogeneous systems. This
results in a equivalent operation of a short period cycle with a small feed flow per cycle,
and a long period cycle with a large feed flow per cycle.
It is therefore determined
impossible to formulate a strategy which can improve the degree of feed conversion, by
only manipulating the vapor flow period duration. This is concluded to most likely be
a consequence of the mathematical model not including any dynamics for the liquid
holdup, since the homogeneous reaction for the MTBE case system deviated from the
above-mentioned behavior.
Additionally, the dimensionless Damköhler number has been evaluated in regards to
potentially being applied in determining the vapor flow rate from the reboiler or catalyst
packing to achieve a desired degree of feed conversion. It is concluded that additional in-
vestigations are needed before a novel design strategy can be presented which incorporates
99
==PAGE:112==
CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
6.0
the Damköhler number for arbitrary cases. It is also concluded that an operation close to
the chemical equilibrium is not necessarily synonymous with an improved total amount
reacted, and may in fact inhibit the rate of reaction due to the increased contribution of
the reverse reaction.
Finally, it is suggested to include a dependency of the liquid holdup into the math-
ematical interpretation of the periodic reactive system before any design strategies can
be formulated. An additional investigation of different formulations of the Damköhler
number for periodic- and reactive operation may result in stronger correlations which can
ultimately be used to formulate novel design strategies for different chemical systems.

==PAGE:125==
Appendix B
MATLAB© R2020b Code
In order to simulate the presented scenarios and run design algorithms it was decided
to implement the derived mathematical expressions in MATLAB© version 2020b. Below
are presented the various scrips and functions employed to produce the results printed in
Section 3.4 and chapter 4.
The main run-scripts located in the root folder are autonomous scripts, which com-
pute the various simulation for the benchmark columns, single tray, complete column
and varying rate simulation studies. For each simulated case the column is formulated as
the custom COLUMN.m structure which contain parameters and methods for evaluating
the column operation. The technical implementation of the solver and thermodynamic
property models are distributed to into various child directories, listed below.
• Verification: run-scripts used to compute the verification studies.
• Thermodynamic models: functions used to compute all the employed thermo-
dynamic properties.
• Simulations: the simulation algorithms used to run the periodic reactive model
and to obtain the pseudo steady-state operation.
• Setup: supporting functions used in computing the simulations.
• Reaction systems: functions used to compute the chemical kinetics for each case
system.
• Mixtures: static scripts containing property model coeﬀicients and constants as-
sociated with each chemical specie in the three case studies.
• Mathematical models: the mathematical models used in the ode15s function for
the complete column, single tray and perturbation tests.
• Design: support functions used to compute the column operation.

80
[~,~,~,obj.nu,~] = getReac([],[],[],[],[],mix,rtype);
81
82

83

84

% COMPUTE THE SHELL BALANCE
107
function [obj] = getShellBalance(obj,pkey,rkey,eps,xpd,xpb)
108
% Load all external flows
109
F
= obj.feed.F;
110
z
= obj.feed.z;
111
U
= obj.extr.SL;
112
W
= obj.extr.SV;
113
114
% Compute the minimum conversion of feed
115
Ftot = sum(F.*z,2);
116
rest = Ftot(rkey).*eps./abs(obj.nu(rkey));
117
Rest = rest.*obj.nu;
118
119
% Compute & export top and bottoms flow (assume W & P fracs.)
120
obj.D = (sum(F.*z(pkey,:),2) + Rest(pkey) - ...
121
sum(U.*0.25,2) - sum(W.*0.25,2) - ...
122
(sum(F,2) + sum(Rest,1) + sum(U) + sum(W)).*xpb)./...
123
(xpd - xpb);
124
obj.B = sum(F,2) + sum(Rest,1) - sum(U,2) - sum(W,2) - obj.D;
125
obj.Rmin = Rest;
126
127
obj.pkey = pkey;
128
obj.rkey = rkey;
129
130
% Ensure that the mass balance is closed:
131
if any(Ftot+Rest<0)
132
warning("Warning: Specified conversion impossible")
133
pause(5);
134
end
135
end

% ADD FEED POINT & COMPUTE ALL PARAMETERS
138
function [obj] = addFeed(obj,nf,F,z,q)
139
140
% Compute isothermal flash calculation.
141
[hL,hV,hF,dHvap,T,x,y] = getFeedFlash(F,z,q,obj.P(nf),obj.mix);
142
143
% Accommodate that the periodic operation is build backwards
144
switch obj.type
145
case "PERIODIC"
146
idx = obj.ntot - nf + 1;
147
case "CONVENTIONAL"
148
idx = nf;
149
end
150
% Update parameters
151
obj.feed.F(idx)
= F;
% [kmol/s]
152
obj.feed.z(:,idx)
= z;
% [-]
153
obj.feed.q(idx)
= q;
% [-]
154
155
% Export computed values
156
obj.feed.x(:,idx)
= x;
% [-]
157
obj.feed.y(:,idx)
= y;
% [-]
158
obj.feed.T(idx)
= T;
% [K]
159
obj.feed.dHvap(idx)
= dHvap;
% [kJ/kmol]
160
obj.feed.hV(idx)
= hV;
% [kJ/kmol]
161
obj.feed.hL(idx)
= hL;
% [kJ/kmol]
162
obj.feed.hF(idx)
= hF;
% [kJ/kmol]
163
end

% ADD EXTERNAL FLOW (EXCLUDING FEEDS)
166
function [obj] = addExternal(obj,idx,n,val)
167
% idx: [1 = SL, 2 = SV, 3 = Q]
168
fns = fieldnames(obj.extr);
169
var = obj.extr.(fns{idx});
170
switch obj.type
171
case "PERIODIC"
172
idx2 = obj.ntot - n + 1;
173
case "CONVENTIONAL"
174
idx2 = n;
175
end
176
var(idx2) = var(idx2) + val;
177
178
% Export computed values
179
obj.extr.(fns{idx}) = var;
180
end

% SPECIFY OPERATIONAL PARAMETER
183
function [obj] = setOperation(obj,RR,V0,mcat,tvfp)
184
obj.RR = RR;
ix
==PAGE:128==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.1
185
obj.V0 = V0;
186
obj.mcat = mcat;
187
if ~isempty(tvfp)
188
obj.tvfp = tvfp;
189
end
190
switch obj.type
191
case "CONVENTIONAL"
192
nt = obj.ntot;
193
194
Rtot = sum(obj.Rmin,1)./sum(obj.rtray).*obj.rtray;
195
FV
= obj.feed.F.*(1-obj.feed.q);
196
FL
= obj.feed.F.*obj.feed.q;
197
198
V = repmat(V0,1,nt);
199
L = repmat(RR.*obj.D,1,nt);
200
201
for i = 2:nt-1
202
V(1,nt-i) = V(1,nt-i+1) + FV(1,nt-i) + ...
203
Rtot(1,nt-i)./2;
204
L(1,i) = L(1,i-1) + FL(1,i) + Rtot(1,i)./2;
205
end
206
207
% From total condenser and partial reboiler
208
V(1) = 0;
209
L(end) = 0;
210
obj.V0 = V;
211
obj.L0 = L;
212
213
case "PERIODIC"
214
obj.BR = obj.V0./obj.B;
215
obj.L0 = obj.RR.*obj.D;
216
end
217
end

function [obj] = updateSimulation(obj,H0,idxFlag)
220
% Load solver options
221
[opsODE]
= getSolverOptions(obj.mix.ncmp,obj.ntot);
222
223
% Compute the pseudo steady state liquid holdup
224
opts
= {obj.iterMin,obj.iterMax,obj.tolPeriodic ,opsODE};
225
[M0]
= getLiqHoldup(H0,obj);
226
[par]
= bldParamCell(obj);
227
[obj.ML]
= simPRDDyn(M0,par,opts,idxFlag);
228
229
% Update the transient holdups and timestamp
230
[obj.t,obj.MV] = ...
231
ode15s(@mdlVFP ,[0,par{9}],obj.ML,opsODE,par,idxFlag);
232
233
% Get the updated external flows
234
[~,obj.L0,obj.BR,obj.B,obj.D] = mdlLFP(obj.MV,par);
235
end

238
239
Data1(:,1) = ["D";"B";"L0";"V0";"RR";"BR"];
240

241
Data1(:,3) = ["[kmol/cycle]","[kmol/cycle]","[kmol/cycle]",...
242
"[kmol/cycle]","[-]","[-]"];

244
Data2(:,1) = [obj.mix.cmpls';obj.mix.cmpls'];
245
Data2(:,2) = [obj.xd;obj.xb];
246
247
tab1 = table(Data1);

252

257
function [obj] = dataAnalysis(obj,idxFlag)
258
259
% RESULTS FROM DYNAMIC BEHAVIOR
260
% - Total amount reacted (cummulative)
261
% - The tray by tray Damköhler number
262
% - (Distance to equilibrium)
263
264
[nc,nt] = size(obj.ML);
265
266
obj.xV
= zeros(size(obj.MV));
267
obj.y
= zeros(size(obj.MV));
268
269
ntime
= length(obj.t);
270
ncons
= sum(obj.nu<0);
271
272
obj.T
= zeros(ntime,nt);
273
obj.V
= zeros(ntime,nt);
274
275
obj.r
= zeros(ntime,nt);
276
obj.kf
= zeros(ntime,nt);
277
obj.Keq = zeros(ntime,nt);
278
279
obj.R
= zeros(ntime,nt);
x
==PAGE:129==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.1
280
obj.Da
= zeros(ntime,nt);
281
obj.Meq = zeros(ntime,nt*nc);
282
dedt
= zeros(ntime,nt);
283
obj.dgc = zeros(ntime,ncons.*nt);
284
idx
= obj.rtray;
285
286
% Compute dynamic parameters
287
for i = 1:ntime
288
289
% Get the liquid holdup at timestamp
290
M = reshape(obj.MV(i,:),nc,nt);
291
x = M./sum(M,1);
292
obj.xV(i,:) = reshape(x,1,[]);
293
294
% Get thermodynamic properties
295
[obj.T(i,:)]
= getTsat(x,obj.P,obj.mix);
296
[K,gamma]
= getVLECons(x,obj.T(i,:),obj.P,obj.mix);
297
[dHvap]
= getdHVap(obj.T(i,:),obj.mix);
298
ytemp
= K.*x;
299
% Get the reaction configurations
300
[obj.r(i,:),obj.kf(i,:),obj.Keq(i,:),~,dHreac] = ...
301
getReac(M,gamma,obj.T(i,:),obj.mcat,obj.rtray,obj.mix,obj.rtype);
302
303
dHR = -dHreac.*obj.r(i,:);
304
FV
= obj.feed.F.*obj.feed.y.*(1-obj.feed.q)./obj.tvfp;
305
dHF = sum(FV,1).*sum(obj.feed.dHvap.*obj.feed.y,1);
306
307
% Cummulative amount reacted on each tray
308
obj.R(i,:) = trapz(obj.t(1:i),obj.r(1:i,:),1);
309
310
% Compute the Vapor flow rate
311
[obj.V(i,:)] = getVaporFlowRate(obj.V0./obj.tvfp,ytemp,dHvap,dHF,dHR,idxFlag);
312
313
obj.y(i,:) = reshape(ytemp ,1,[]);
314
315
% Compute the Damköhler number
316
if obj.mix.name == "MTBE"
317
obj.Da(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:)./obj.V(i,:).*obj.rtray.*sum(M,1);
318
else
319
obj.Da(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:)./obj.V(i,:).*obj.rtray;
320
end
321
% Compute the distance to equilibrium (issues when trays not reactive ??)
322
[~,dedt(i,idx == 1),~] = ...
323
getEqComp(M(:,idx == 1),obj.P(idx == 1),...
324
obj.mix,obj.nu,obj.mcat,obj.rtype);
325
326
% EXTENT IS ONLY GUARENTEED SINGLE ROW:
327
Meqtemp
= M + obj.nu.*dedt(i,:);
328
convtemp
= -obj.nu(obj.nu<0).*dedt(i,:)./M(obj.nu<0,:);
329
obj.Meq(i,:) = reshape(Meqtemp,1,nc*nt);
330
obj.dgc(i,:) = reshape(convtemp ,1,ncons*nt);
331
332
end
333
% RESULTS WITHOUT TIME DEPENDENCY
334
% Compute distillate and bottoms composition
335
% (M at i = ntime above is equal to MV)
336
obj.xd = M(:,nt)./sum(M(:,nt),1);
337
obj.xb = M(:,1)./sum(M(:,1),1);
338
339
% Reactant conversions
340
out = obj.xd(obj.nu<0).*obj.D + obj.xb(obj.nu<0).*obj.B;
341
in
= sum(obj.feed.F.*obj.feed.z(obj.nu<0,:),2);
342
obj.conv
= 1 - out./in;
343
344
end
345
346
function [obj,Da1,Da2] = getDamkohler(obj)
347
ntime = length(obj.t);
348
for i = 1:ntime
349
M = sum(reshape(obj.MV(i,:),obj.mix.ncmp,[]),1);
350
if obj.mix.name == "MTBE"
351
Da1(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:).*M./obj.V(i,:);
352
Da2(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:).*obj.tvfp;
353
else
354
Da1(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:)./obj.V(i,:);
355
Da2(i,:) = obj.kf(i,:).*obj.tvfp./M;
356
end
357
end
358
end

Verify the MTBE reaction system
1
% VERIFICATION STUDY: RATE KINETICS OF THE MTBE SYSTEM
2
% Clear the workspace, and command window.
3
clear variables; close all; clc
4
addAllDirs2Path()
5

%% LOAD CASE SETUP & MIXTURE
7
mix
= MTBE();
8
MIR
= 2;
% [MeOH/i-Bu]
Ratio of feeds
9
T
= 353.15;
% [K]
Fixed temperature
10
wt
= 0.01;
% [wt%]
Weight percent sulpheric acid
11
tmax
= 200*60;
% [s]
Simulation time
12
Rgas
= 1.987e3;
% [kcal/mol/K]
Ideal gas constant
13
14
% Sulpheric acid catalyst
15
MwH2SO4 = 98.07848; % [kmol/m3]
16
17
% Get the initial holdup composition
18
x = zeros(mix.ncmp,1);
19
x(1) = 1/(MIR + 1);
20
x(2) = 1 - x(1);
21
22
% Perform a linear fit of adjusted Arrehnius data
23
csvfile
= "\Raw data\Al-Jarallah 1988\MTBERAW.csv";
24
tab
= readtable(append(pwd,csvfile));
25
[p1,rsq1]
= getLinFit(tab.k1_x,tab.k1_y);
% k1 data
26
[p2,rsq2]
= getLinFit(tab.k2_x,tab.k2_y);
% k2 data
27
28
% Compute the mass of sulpheric catalyst
29
Msol = sum(x.*mix.Mw,1);
% [kg]
30
mcat = Msol./(1-wt).*wt./MwH2SO4;
% [kmol] Acid
31
32
% Two sets of Arrhenius parameters:
33
A
= [16.43e8, exp(p1(2)); 1.25e20, exp(p2(2))];
34
Ea
= [12600, -p1(1)*Rgas; 31100, -p2(1)*Rgas];
35
36
optODE
= odeset('RelTol',1e-6,'AbsTol',1e-6);
37
[tog,Mog]
= ode113(@ffun,[0,tmax],x,optODE,mix,T,mcat,A(:,1),Ea(:,1));
38
[tnew,Mnew] = ode113(@ffun,[0,tmax],x,optODE,mix,T,mcat,A(:,2),Ea(:,2));
39
40
% Compute the simulation using published parameters
41
res.og.t = tog';
42
res.og.M = Mog';
43
res.og.x = Mog'./sum(Mog,2)';
44
Tvec = repmat(T,1,length(tog'));
45
res.og.C = res.og.M./sum(res.og.M./getVolMolarLiq(res.og.x,Tvec,mix),1);
46
47
% Compute the simulation using the adjusted parameters
48
res.new.t = tnew';
49
res.new.M = Mnew';
50
res.new.x = Mnew'./sum(Mnew,2)';
51
Tvec = repmat(T,1,length(tnew'));
52

function [dMdt] = ffun(t,M,mix,T,mcat,A,Ea)
59
x = M./sum(M,1);
60
61
V = sum(M./getVolMolarLiq(x,T,mix),1); % [m3]
62
mcat = mcat./V;
63
64
nu
= [-1;-1;1];
65
66
% Rate order of sepcific species
67
a = 0.5; b = 0.5; c = 1;
d = 1.5;
68
69
% Concentration of species [kmol/m3] = [mol/L]
70
Vol
= sum(M./getVolMolarLiq(x,T,mix));
% [m3] (pure species)
71
C
= M./Vol;
% [kmol/m3] = [mol/L]
72
C(C<0)
= 0;
73
74
% Forward and backwards reaction constants [kmol/min/m3]
75
k1
= (mcat.^d).*A(1).*exp(-Ea(1)./(1.987.*T))./3600;
76
k2
= (mcat.^d).*A(2).*exp(-Ea(2)./(1.987.*T))./3600;
77
k
= [k1;k2];
78
79
% Rate expression [mol/min*kgCat]
80
% Convert to units [kmol/min]
81
r = (k1.*(C(1,:).^b).*(C(2,:).^a) ...
82
- k2.*(C(3,:).^c)).*1.*Vol;
83
84
R = r.*nu;
85
dMdt = +R;
86
end

Wilson
1
function [gamma] = getWILSON(x,T,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molefracs
5
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
6
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
7
%
8
%
gamma : [-]
{nc*nt}
Vector of liquid activity coefs
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
Compute the liquid activity coefficients using the Wilson method.
12
%
Due to the binary interaction parameters being determined from
13
%
independent sources several methods are used in computing the
14
%
Wilson parameters. This is accounted for by the "type" flag which
15
%
is mixture dependent.
16
17
% Define the result storage
18
[nc,nt] = size(x);
19
gamma
= ones(nc,nt);
20
vm
= (mix.liq.vm./mix.liq.vm')';
21
22
% Iterate for each tray, liquid composition.
23
for i = 1:nt
24
switch mix.liq.type
25
case 1
26
lambda = vm.*exp(-mix.liq.a./(R*T(i)));
27
case 2
28
lambda = vm.*exp(-mix.liq.a./T(i));
29
case 3
30
lambda = vm.*exp(mix.liq.a + mix.liq.b./T(i));
31
otherwise
32
error("Incorrect 'type' specification")
33
end
34
sr
= lambda*x(:,i);
%Constant sum 1
35
36
% Compute and store the activity coefficients
37
gamma(:,i) = real(1 - log(sr) - sum(lambda.*x(:,i)./sr,1)');
38
gamma(:,i) = exp(gamma(:,i));
39
end
40
end
NRTL
1
function [gamma] = getNRTL(x,T,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molfracs
5
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
6
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
7
%
8
%
gamma : [-]
{nc*nt}
Vector of liquid activity coefs
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
Compute the liquid activity coefficients using the NRTL method.
12
%
The function is designed to account for different binary
13
%
interactions parameter reported in different formats. The
14
%
selected method is selected in the mixture script using the
15
%
'type' flag
16
17
% Define the result storage
18
[nc,nt] = size(x);
19
gamma
= ones(nc,nt);
20
21
% Iterate for each list of liquid compositions
22
for i = 1:nt
23
switch mix.liq.type
24
case 1
25
tau = mix.liq.dg./T(i);
26
case 2
27
tau = mix.liq.dg./(8.314*T(i));
28
case 3
29
tau = mix.liq.a+mix.liq.b./T(i);
30
otherwise
31
error("Incorrect 'type' specification")
32
end
33
G
= exp(-mix.liq.alpha.*tau);
34
CS1 = sum(G.*x(:,i),1);
% Constant sum 1
35
CS2 = sum(G.*tau.*x(:,i),1);
% Constant sum 2
36
37
% Compute and store the activity coefficients
38
gamma(:,i) = CS2./CS1 + ...
39
sum(G'.*x(:,i)./CS1'.*(tau'-(CS2./CS1)'),1);
40
gamma(:,i) = exp(gamma(:,i))';
41
end
42
end

Saturation Pressure
1
function [Psat] = getPsat(T,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
5
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
6
%
7
%
Psat
: [bar]
{1*nt}
Vector of saturation pressures
8
%
9
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
10
%
Using the Antoine or the Extended Antoine equations the saturation
11
%
pressure of the pure components in a specified mixture is
12
%
calculated at arbitrary temperatures.
13
14
% Get the stored coefficients
15
params = mix.gas.vapcons;
16
switch mix.gas.type
17
case "ANTOINE"
18
A = params(:,1);
19
B = params(:,2);
20
C = params(:,3);
21
Psat = A - B./(T + C);
% [bar]
22
Psat = 10.^Psat;
% [bar]
23
case "ASPEN"
24
C1 = params(:,1);
25
C2 = params(:,2);
26
C3 = params(:,3);
27
C4 = params(:,4);
28
C5 = params(:,5);
29
C6 = params(:,6);
30
C7 = params(:,7);
31
32
% Extended Antoine equation (ASPEN PLUS V11)
33
Psat = C1 + C2./(C3+T) + C4.*T + C5.*log(T) + C6.*T.^(C7);
34
Psat = exp(Psat); % [bar]
35
otherwise
36
error("Incorrect 'method' selection")
37
end
38
end

function [Tsat] = getTsat(x,P,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molefracs
5
%
P
: [bar]
{1*nt}
Vector of pressures
6
%
mix
: [NaN]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
7
%
8
%
Tsat
: [K]
{nc*nt}
Vector of saturation temperatures
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
Using the modified Raoult's law, the temperature is found by
12
%
satisfying "y = K.*x" at a specified pressure value.
13
14
% RESULT STORAGE
15
[~,nt]
= size(x);
16
Tsat
= zeros(1,nt);
17
18
% Fzero options
19
options = optimset('Display','off','TolX',1e-16);
20
21
% ITERATIVE SCHEME WITH @FZERO FASTER THAN MATRIX SCHEME WITH @FSOLVE
22
for i = 1:nt
23
xi = x(:,i);
24
Pi = P(1,i);
25
26
% [x,fval,exitflag,output] access for debugging:
27
[Tsat(1,i),~,~] = fzero(@(T)obj(xi,T,Pi,mix),298,options);
28
end
29
end
30
function [ceq] = obj(x,T,P,mix)
31
%
OBJECTIVE FUNCTION
32
%
=================================================================
33
34
% Compute vapor-liquid-equilibrium constant from mod. Raoult's law
35
[K,~] = getVLECons(x,T,P,mix);
36
ceq = ones(size(T)) - sum(K.*x,1);
37
end
Heat of Vaporization
1
function [Hvap] = getdHVap(T,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
================================================================
4
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
5
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
6
%
xx
==PAGE:139==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.3
7
%
Hvap
: [kJ/kmol]
{nc*nt}
Heat of pure specie vaporization
8
%
9
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
10
%
The heat of vaporization is calculated from a correlation
11
%
employed by Aspen. The limit is Tc. If a value is computed
12
%
outside the correlation boundaries, which can occur due to
13
%
numerical errors, the heat is set to zero. This corresponds to
14
%
the value at T = Tc.
15
16
% Get mixture specific constants
17
params = mix.gas.dHvap;
18
C1 = params(:,1);
19
C2 = params(:,2);
20
C3 = params(:,3);
21
C4 = params(:,4);
22
23
% Reduced temperature [-]
24
Tr = T./mix.Tc;
25
26
% Heat of vaporization []
27
Hvap = C1.*(1-Tr).^(C2 + C3.*Tr + C4.*Tr.^2);
% [kJ/kmol]
28
Hvap(Tr>=1) = 0;
29
end
Enthalpy
1
function [hL,hV,dHvap] = getEnthalpy(x,y,T,Tsat,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{struct}
Description
3
%
================================================================
4
%
x
: [mol/mol]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molfracs
5
%
y
: [mol/mol]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of vapor molfracs
6
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
7
%
Tsat
: [-]
{1*nt}
Cell of parameters
8
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Cell of parameters
9
%
10
%
hL
: [kJ/kmol]
{1*nt}
Liquid flow enthalpy
11
%
hV
: [kJ/kmol]
{1*nt}
Vapor flow enthalpy
12
%
dHvap : [kJ/kmol]
{nc*nt}
Liquid flow enthalpy
13
%
14
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
15
%
The enthalpy is calculated assuming pressure effects can be
16
%
neglected. For the purposes of this project the enthalpy around
17
%
saturation temperatures are only required. However, the function
18
%
is structured to accomodate superheated vapor and subcooled
19
%
liquid aswell.
20
21
% Load parameters
22
CpL
= mix.liq.CpL; % [kJ/kmol/K] Liquid heat capacity
23
CpV
= mix.gas.CpV; % [kJ/kmol/K] Vapor heat capacity
24
25
% Reference state @ 298.15 [K] with h0 = 0 [kJ/kmol]
26
[nc,nt] = size(x);
27
Tref
= 298.15;
28
h0
= zeros(nc,nt);
29
dHvap
= h0;
30
hi
= h0;
31
Hi
= h0;
32
33
for i = 1:nt
34
if T(i) <= Tsat(i)
35
% Heat of varporization
36
dHvap(:,i) = getdHVap(T(i),mix); % [kJ/kmol]
37
% Liquid enthalpy at saturation temperature
38
hi(:,i) = h0(:,i) + CpL.*(T(i) - Tref);
39
Hi(:,i) = hi(:,i) + dHvap(:,i);
40
41
elseif T(i) > Tsat(i)
42
% Heat of varporization
43
dHvap(:,i) = getdHVap(Tsat(i),mix); % [kJ/kmol]
44
% Liquid enthalpy at saturation temperature
45
hi(:,i) = h0(:,i) + CpL.*(Tsat(i) - Tref);
46
Hi(:,i) = hi(:,i) + CpV.*(T(i) - Tsat(i)) + dHvap(:,i);
47
end
48
end
49
50
% Computing the combined enthalpies
51
hL = sum(x.*hi,1); % [kJ/kmol]
52
hV = sum(y.*Hi,1); % [kJ/kmol]
53
end
Vapor Liquid Equilibrium Constant
1
function [K,gamma] = getVLECons(x,T,P,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molfracs
5

Liquid Molar Density
1
function [rhoLiq] = getVolMolarLiq(x,T,mix)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molfracs
5
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
6
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
7
%
8
%
rhoLiq: [kmol/m3]
{nc*nt}
Molar volume of pure liq species
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
The molar volume is calculated from a correlation employed by
12
%
Aspen. The limit is Tc. If a value is computed outside the
13
%
correlation boundaries, which can occur due to numerical errors,
14
%
the molar volume is set equal to the maximum value of T = Tc.
15
16
% Get mixture specific constants
17
params = mix.Vm;
18
C1 = params(:,1);
19
C2 = params(:,2);
20
C3 = params(:,3);
21
C4 = params(:,4);
22
C5 = params(:,5);
23
24
% Check if water is part of mixture (unique expression implemented)
25
comp = mix.cmpls;
26
27
if sum(comp == "Water") > 0
28
29
rhoLiq = zeros(size(x));
30
Tr
= T./647.13; % [K] Critical Temperature of water
31
32
idx1 = comp=="Water";
33
idx2 = ~idx1;
34
35
% Molar density of water [kmol/m3]
36
rhoLiq(idx1,:) = C1(idx1) + C2(idx1).*(1-Tr).^0.35 + ...
37
C3(idx1).*(1 - Tr).^(2/3) +...
38
C4(idx1).*(1 - Tr) + C5(idx1).*(1 - Tr).^(4./3);
39
40
% Molar density of pure components (not water) [kmol/m3]
41
rhoLiq(idx2,:) = C1(idx2)./...
42
(C2(idx2).^(1 + (1-T./C3(idx2)).^C4(idx2)));
43
else
44
% Molar density of pure components (not water) [kmol/m3]
45
rhoLiq = C1./(C2.^(1 + (1-T./C3).^C4));
46
end
47
48
% Ensure the function does not produce imaginary of nan solutions
49
Tmax
= repmat(mix.Tc,1,length(T));
50
rhoSupCrit = zeros(size(x));
51
idx = comp == "Water";
52
rhoSupCrit(~idx,:) = C1(~idx)./(C2(~idx).^(1 + (1-Tmax(~idx,:)./...
53
C3(~idx)).^C4(~idx)));
54
rhoSupCrit(idx,:) = C1(idx);
55
56
% Find solutions with an imaginary number and replace with maximum
xxii
==PAGE:141==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.3
57
rhoLiq(imag(rhoLiq)>0) = rhoSupCrit(imag(rhoLiq)>0);
58
end

function [jac] = getJacSparsePatrut(nc,nt)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
nc
: [-]
{int}
Number of unique species in mixture
5
%
nt
: [-]
{int}
Number of trays in column
6
%
7
%
jac
: [-]
{nc*nt^2}
Sparse jacobian matrix for Patrut mdl
8
%
9
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
10
%
Compute the sparse jacobian matrix for the mathematical model presented
11
%
by Patrut. The sparse matrix is used in non-stiff solvers to
12
%
preallocate variable/function gradients not equal to zero. By
13
%
predefining these combinations denoted by a '1' it can speed up the
14
%
ODE solvers tremendeously.
15
%
16
%
| df1dx1, df1dx2, df1dx3, .... df1xnvar |
17
%
jac =
| df2dx1, df2dx2, ...
|
18
%
| df3dx1, ...
|
19
20
% All function/variable combinations
21
nvar
= nc*nt;
22
jac
= zeros(nvar,nvar);
23
24
% All species on the tray above and below possibly affect the given tray:
25
jac(1:nc,1:nc) = 1;
26
27
for i = 2:1:nt
28
ridx = ((i-1)*nc + 1):(i*nc);
% Update row index
29
cidx = ((i-2)*nc + 1):(i*nc);
% Update col index
30
jac(ridx,cidx) = 1;
31
end
32
end
Linear fit
1
function [p,rsq] = getLinFit(x,y)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
x
: [several]
{ndata}
Data points part 'x'
5
%
y
: [several]
{ndata}
Data points part 'y'
6
%
7
%
p
: [several]
{2*float}
Vector polynomial coefficients
8
%
rsq
: [-]
{float}
Residual square error
xxvi
==PAGE:145==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.5
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
Produce a linear fit to data. Used to fit the adjusted Arrhenius
12
%
parameters from the published plot by Al-Jarallah 1988.
13
14
% Fit first order polynomial
15
p = polyfit(x,y,1);
16
17
% Get fitted 'y' from polynomial and determine difference from 'y' data.
18
yfit = polyval(p,x);
19
yresid = y - yfit;
20
21
% Sum the square difference and a divide by the 'y' data variance
22
SSresid = sum(yresid.^2);
23
SStotal = (length(y)-1) * var(y);
24
rsq = 1 - SSresid/SStotal;
25
26
% Print fitted polynomial and residual square error
27
fprintf("Linear fit: y = %.3fx + %.3f\n", p(1), p(2));
28
fprintf("R2: %.3f\n", rsq);
29
end

20

= [];

case 1

% NOTE THAT IN THIS MODEL, ALPHA = LIQUID CONCENTRATION
30
% 1: Homogeneous reaction by Al Jarallah et. al. (1988)
31
% This reaction system is homogeneous with sulferic acid catalyst
32
% which is reported in "mix.mcat" in units of [mol/L].
33
34
% Exothermic heat of reaction [kJ/kmol] & stoichiometry
35
dHreac
= -37.2e3;
36
nu
= [-1;-1;1];
37
38
if ~isempty(x) &&
~isempty(T) && ~isempty(mix)
39
% Rate order of sepcific species
40
a = 0.5; b = 0.5; c = 1;
d = 1.5;
41
42
% Concentration of species [kmol/m3] = [mol/L]
43
vol
= sum(M./getVolMolarLiq(x,T,mix),1);
% [m3]
44
C
= M./vol;
% [kmol/m3] = ([mol/L])
45
C(C<0)
= 0;
46
47
% Forward and backwards rate constants [L^1.5/mol^1.5/min]
48
kd1 = 6.929e9.*exp(-13231./(1.987.*T))./3600;
% (h = 3600 s)
49
kd2 = 1.250e20.*exp(-31166./(1.987.*T))./3600;
% (h = 3600 s)
50
51
% Catalyst adjusted Forward and backwards rate constant [1/s]
xxx
==PAGE:149==
APPENDIX B. MATLAB© R2020B CODE
B.6

= (mcat.^d).*kd1;

k2
= (mcat.^d).*kd2;
54

= k1;
55

57
% Keq = exp(4254.05./T - 10.0982 + 0.2667.*log(T));
58
Keq = k1./k2;
59

61
% Convert to units [kmol/s] by miultiplying 'vol'
62

63
rtray.*vol;
64
end

otherwise
66
error("!!! WARNING: INCORRECT REACTION MODEL SELECTED !!!")
67
end

Patrut vapor flow model
1
function [dMdt,Tsat,y,r,kf,Keq,V] = mdlVFP(t,ML,par,flag)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
t
: [s]
{float}
Time used to solve the ODE
5
%
ML
: [kmol]
{1*nc*nt}
Vector of liquid molar holdups
6
%
par
: [-]
{1*npar}
Cell vector with parameters
7
%
flags : [-]
{1*nflag}
Flags used to select design aspects
8
%
9
%
dMdt
: [kmol/s]
{nc*nt}
The mass-balance gradient
10
%
Tsat
: [K]
{1*nt}
Saturation temperature
11
%
y
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Vapor mole fraction
12
%
r
: [kmol/s]
{1*nt}
Rate of reaction
13
%
kf
: [kmol/s]
{1*nt}
Forward rate constant
14
%
Keq
: [-]
{1*nt}
Equilibrium constant from rate expr.
15
%
V
: [kmol/s]
{1*nt}
Vapor flow rate
16
%
17
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
18
%
Compute a pseudo steady-statefor the periodic reactive distilaltion
19
%
unit by simulating the dynamics untill they converge where the liquid
20
%
holdup after the liquid flow period is identical to that in the
21
%
beginning of the vapor flow period.
22
23
% Load required parameters
24
[nt,nc,mix,rtray,mcat,P,tvfp,V0,F,q,yF,dHvapF,rtype] = ...
25
par{[1,2,3,4,5,6,9,10,11,12,14,15,16]};

39

= getReac(ML,gamma,Tsat,mcat,rtray,mix,rtype);
40

= k(1,:);

42

48

52

54

+ FV(:,1:nt-2) + R(:,2:nt-1);

21

= MV(:,nt) - (D + L).*x(:,nt);

function [Meq,dedt,conv] = getEqComp(M,P,mix,nu,mcat,rtype)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{struct}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
M
: [mol]
{nc*nt}
Molar holdup
5
%
P
: [K]
{1}
Vector of temperatures
6
%
mix
: [NaN]
{struct}
Structure of parametrers used in VLE
7
%
8
%
Meq
: [mol]
{nc*nt}
Molar holdup at equilibrium
9
%
dedt
: [mol]
{1,nt}
Extent of reaction
10
%
conv
: [-]
{nc,nt}
Conversion of reactants
11
%
12
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
13
%
Given initial conditions and reaction specification , calculate the
14
%
extent of reaction required to reach and equilibrium composition.
15
%
It is assumed that the mixture is at phase equilibrium.
16
17
% Produce an initial guess:
18
[nc,np] = size(M);
19
eps0
= max(M)/3;
20
21
% Solver settings
22
opts
= optimoptions(@lsqnonlin,'Display','off','TolFun',1e-18);
23
dedt
= zeros(1,np);
24
lb
= zeros(1,np);
25
ub
= M./(-nu);
26
ub
= min(ub(nu<0,:),[],1);
27
28
ub(ub<0) = 0;
29
30
for i = 1:np
31
% Update parameter cells
32
par = {M(:,i),P,mix,nu,mcat,rtype};
33
34
% Solve for extent of reaction
35
[res,~,~,~,~] = lsqnonlin(@obj,ub(i)*0.99,lb(i),ub(i),opts,par);
36
dedt(i) = res;
37
end
38
39
% COMPUTE EQUILIBRIUM HOLDUP & CONVERSION OF REACTANTS
40
Meq
= M + nu.*dedt;
41
conv
= -nu(nu<0).*dedt./M(nu<0);
42
43
end
44
function [ceq] = obj(dedt,par)
45
% OBJECTIVE FUNCTION: USE PROD(ALPHA) = KEQ TO FIND EXTENT
46
% The solution is obtained iterative i.e. only vector operations
47
[M,P,mix,nu,mcat,rtype] = par{:};
48
49
% Update guess for equilibrium composition
50
M = M + nu.*dedt;
51
x = M./sum(M,1);
52
53
% Compute thermodynamic properties
54
Tsat
= getTsat(x,P,mix);
55
[~,gamma]
= getVLECons(x,Tsat,P,mix);
56
alpha
= gamma.*x;
57
[~,~,Keq,~,~] = getReac(M,gamma,Tsat,mcat,1,mix,rtype);
58
59
% EQUILIBRIUM CONSTRAINT
60
ceq = abs(prod(alpha.^nu,1) - Keq);
61
end

Linear fit
1
function [p,rsq] = getLinFit(x,y)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
x
: [several]
{ndata}
Data points part 'x'
5
%
y
: [several]
{ndata}
Data points part 'y'
6
%
7
%
p
: [several]
{2*float}
Vector polynomial coefficients
8
%
rsq
: [-]
{float}
Residual square error
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
Produce a linear fit to data. Used to fit the adjusted Arrhenius
12
%
parameters from the published plot by Al-Jarallah 1988.
13
14
% Fit first order polynomial
15
p = polyfit(x,y,1);
16
17
% Get fitted 'y' from polynomial and determine difference from 'y' data.
18
yfit = polyval(p,x);
19
yresid = y - yfit;
20
21
% Sum the square difference and a divide by the 'y' data variance
22
SSresid = sum(yresid.^2);
23
SStotal = (length(y)-1) * var(y);
24
rsq = 1 - SSresid/SStotal;
25
26
% Print fitted polynomial and residual square error
27
fprintf("Linear fit: y = %.3fx + %.3f\n", p(1), p(2));
28
fprintf("R2: %.3f\n", rsq);
29
end

function [vweep,h,v,Dcol] = getWeepingVelocity(M,V,P,par)
2
%
SYNTAX: [unit]
{format}
Description
3
%
=====================================================================
4
%
x
: [-]
{nc*nt}
Matrix of liquid molfracs
5
%
T
: [K]
{1*nt}
Vector of temperatures
6
%
mix
: [-]
{struct}
Structure of thermo properties
7
%
8
%
gamma : [-]
{nc*nt}
Vector of liquid activity coefs
9
%
10
%
NOTES & OBJECTIVE:
11
%
The Eduljee correlation is used to compute the liquid height, vapor
12
%
verlocity, weeping velocity and
column diameter which satisfy the
13
%
input holdup, flow rate and pressure. The maximum column diameter
14
%
and hole diameter has to be presdetermined
15
16
R = 8.314; % [1e3(Pa*m3)/kmol*K]
17
18
mix = par{1};
19
20
% Input constraints for flexibility
21
hmax
= par{2}; % [mm]
22
Dclim
= par{3}; % [m]
23
alpha
= par{4}; % [%]
24
dhole
= par{5}; % [mm]
25
26
[nc,nt] = size(M);
27
28
% Compute all preliminary values
29
M = reshape(M,mix.ncmp,[]);
30
x = M./sum(M,1);
31
Dc = zeros(1,nt);
32
vweep = zeros(1,nt);
33
hfin = zeros(1,nt);
34
35
[T] = getTsat(x,P,mix);
36
[K,~] = getVLECons(x,T,P,mix);
37
y = K.*x;
38
39
rhoL = getVolMolarLiq(x,T,mix);
% [kmol/m^3]
40
rhoV = P./(R.*T).*sum(mix.Mw.*y,1).*1e2;
% [kg/m^3]
41
42
opts
= optimoptions(@lsqnonlin,'Display','off','TolFun',1e-18);
43
Dc0
= ones(1,nt).*Dclim(1).*1.5;
44
lb
= ones(1,nt).*Dclim(1); % []
45
ub
= ones(1,nt).*Dclim(2);
46
47
for i = 1:nt
48
% Update par
49
par = {M(:,i),rhoL(:,i),hmax};
50
51
[res,~,~,~,~] = lsqnonlin(@obj,Dc0(i),lb(i),ub(i),opts,par);
52
Dc(i) = res;
53
end
54
55
% Determine the actual column diameter
56
Dcol = max(Dc(2:nt-1));
57
Acol = pi./4.*Dcol.^2;
58
59
h = sum(M./rhoL ,1).*4./(pi.*(Dcol.^2)).*1e3;
% [mm]
60
K = 33.9025 + ((-1.4285e5 - 33.9025)./(1 + (h./(1.18e-9)).^0.4295));
61
62
vweep(2:nt-1) = (K(1:nt-2) - 0.9.*(25.4 - dhole))./sqrt(rhoV(1:nt-2)); % [m/s]
63
v = V.*R.*T./(P.*alpha.*Acol).*1e-2; % [m/s]
64
end

Appendix C
Simulations with Alternative
Indexing
In this appendix are simulation results presented, which have been produced using a
model implementation that includes the index error presented in the original article by
Patrut [1]. Three case studies are presented below, consisting of a periodic simulation
figure and summary table which display the system behavior and column operation per-
formance.
